2026 Lemley Lecture Featuring AI Pioneer Yann LeCun

Brown University(83.8K) ·99:55 ·1.7K views · 2026-04-02
Yann LeCun (AI Pioneer)
Yann LeCun argues that current Large Language Models (LLMs) will never achieve human-level intelligence; instead, the future of AI lies in "World Models" that use Joint Embedding Predictive Architectures (JEPA) to understand physical reality and perform hierarchical planning.
Yann LeCun argues that current Large Language Models (LLMs) will never achieve human-level intelligence; instead, the future of AI lies in "World Models" that use Joint Embedding Predictive Architectures (JEPA) to understand physical reality and perform hierarchical planning. -...

TL;DR

Yann LeCun argues that current Large Language Models (LLMs) will never achieve human-level intelligence; instead, the future of AI lies in "World Models" that use Joint Embedding Predictive Architectures (JEPA) to understand physical reality and perform hierarchical planning.

The Failure of the LLM Paradigm

  • Data Inefficiency: Language data is insufficient for human-level intelligence.
    • LLMs are trained on roughly $10^{14}$ bytes of text, representing 400,000 years of reading.
    • A four-year-old child has processed $10^{14}$ bytes of visual data in just 16,000 hours of wake time.
  • Moravec’s Paradox: AI can pass the bar exam but cannot perform basic physical tasks, such as clearing a dinner table or driving a car with the efficiency of a teenager (20 hours of practice).
  • The "Agentic" Flaw: Current systems are not truly agentic because they cannot predict the consequences of their actions.
    • Scaling LLMs to reach human-level intelligence is described by LeCun as "complete BS."
    • Autoregressive token prediction is computationally inferior to inference via search and optimization.

World Models and JEPA Architectures

  • Joint Embedding Predictive Architecture (JEPA): LeCun proposes moving away from generative models (which try to predict every pixel) toward joint embedding.
    • Abstract Representations: JEPAs predict the "state" of the world in an abstract space, ignoring unpredictable details (like the exact texture of a wall) to focus on relevant outcomes.
    • Non-Collapsing Systems: Tools like SigReg (developed with Brown faculty Randall Balestriero) help maximize information content and prevent the system from "collapsing" into useless, constant representations.
  • Hierarchical Planning: Human intelligence relies on decomposing goals.
    • A trip from NYU to Paris involves subgoals (getting to the airport) and lower-level actions (hailing a taxi, walking).
    • Current AI cannot yet perform this type of multi-level planning; it remains a primary research frontier.
  • Inherent Safety: Systems built on world models are intrinsically safer because they can use "guardrail objectives" during the planning phase to reject actions with dangerous predicted outcomes.

Industry Impact and the Future of Ami Labs

  • Industrial Applications: The first successful world models will likely operate "behind the curtain" in B2B sectors.
    • Use Cases: Optimizing jet engines, refineries, chemical plants, and drug production by predicting system states via thousands of sensors.
    • Scientific Discovery: AI can learn "phenomenological models" for complex systems that defy simple reductionist equations, such as discovering materials for more efficient catalysis or higher refractive indices.
  • The Open Source Advantage: LeCun's new company, Ami Labs, intends to follow the open research model established at Meta’s FAIR.
    • Open source allows for "exploratory research" through resident PhD students and academic collaboration, which LeCun credits for the success of models like Llama.

Guidance for Education and Career

  • Focus on Fundamentals: Students should prioritize subjects with a long "shelf life."
    • Core Recommendation: Given a choice between mobile app programming and quantum mechanics, choose quantum mechanics.
    • Fundamental physics and math provide the framework for learning new technologies as they emerge.
  • Learning to Learn: As technological progress accelerates, the ability to adapt to new situatons "zero-shot" is the ultimate competitive advantage for both humans and machines.

Notable Quotes

"The people who think that by training LLMs and making them bigger... we're going to reach human intelligence are deluded. It's complete BS." - on the current AI investment bubble.
"Intelligence is the ability to accomplish new tasks you've never been exposed to and solve new problems without any prior training... fast adaptation in front of new situations." - on the definition of true AI.
"The most incomprehensible thing about the world is that the world is comprehensible." - quoting Albert Einstein on the power of abstract modeling.
"Abandon generative models... abandon reinforcement learning. Minimize its use because it's so damn inefficient." - on the necessary shifts in AI research.
Okay, great. Wonderful to see a packed room here. Very excited. For those of you I haven't had the pleasure of meeting, I'm Frank Doyle. I'm the 14th Provost here at Brown University, where I'm also a professor of engineering and a professor of neuroscience. And it's my great pleasure to welcome you to our annual Lemley Lecture. I'm going to give you just a quick explanation of what the lecture series is. This is the Lemley Family Leadership Lecture Series, and we invite exceptional leaders who are preeminent in their field to come to campus, engage and inspire the university community. We established this in 2020 through a generous gift made by Wayne C. Lemley, class of 1980 Brown PhD. Our series features highly accomplished scholars, thought leaders, policymakers, and practitioners who will inform, educate, and challenge, advancing Brown's commitment to promoting a vibrant intellectual environment. And to introduce today's speaker, I'd like to call up to the podium our Associate Provost for AI, Professor Michael Littman. Thanks, everybody. I'm so glad everybody could make it today. I actually didn't think this was going to work, that you'd see right through it—that Yann is not actually here. As an April Fools joke, we just thought it would be really funny to get everybody to come out here. This is CS1413: Advanced Prompting for LLMs. All right, so first slide... no, okay. All right, that was a joke. All right, so hi everybody. As Frank said, my name is Michael Littman. I am our... he was the 14th Provost, which seems like maybe not enough provosts given how long Brown has been around, but I am the first Associate Provost for Artificial Intelligence, which seems like maybe that is enough Associate Provost of Artificial Intelligence. But I wanted to give everybody a chance to kind of get to know a little bit about Yann before he comes on and tells us about his work. So, I actually first heard Yann speak in 1988 when he was just one year out of his PhD. In his graduate work, he developed the mathematical underpinnings for back propagation. That's the algorithm that actually makes it possible to train these giant neural networks, or then known as connectionist systems, that underlie chatbots and thousands of other recent innovations today. So not everybody knows that Yann deserves credit for this, but I do because that's what his talk was about in 1988. And so it was really cool to learn about that sort of in the moment, while this stuff was actually happening in the early days. That was like the second wave of neural networks. We're now in what we could view as the third wave of neural networks. So in the years since, I've crossed paths with Yann many many times. I've seen him give many invited talks, all of which have been equal parts inspiring and impressive. The inspiring part is that he's a brilliant researcher and his ideas are incredibly deep and insightful. He presents new approaches that are just so cool that you just have to go off and try them yourself, right? So you leave the room—at least when you're in a room full of computer scientists—and everybody wants to run off and think: is that true? Can I actually try that? The impressive part of his talks is that he actually is a consummate tinkerer, and he often creates live demos to accompany his talks. And if you've ever done a live demo, it is a very dangerous thing to do, and I've seen him do it multiple times. Maybe we'll see one today? He says no. All right, which I think is the right thing to do, because they can go very badly. So now if you have followed Yann online, you might have seen that he has very high standards. He will sometimes complain about the status quo, but he's also very very generous. Just as often as he points out the flaws that he sees, he also creates solutions. For example, if he's dissatisfied with a piece of technology, he'll create a custom-built alternative. And maybe in the case of creating his own programming language, or his own document and image presentation software, it's really high quality stuff. And not only does he create this for his own use because he doesn't like what's out there, but he provides it for other people to use as well. Often that's set up as open software, something for everybody to share and everybody to use, because that's who he is and that's what he believes. Throughout his career, he has collected a wide variety of distinctions, and I'd like to mention two that might be relevant to the talk, but I'm not sure what the talk is on, so we'll see. The first is that he is a Turing Award winner, so that's the closest thing that computing has to a Nobel Prize. And the award cites his conceptual and engineering breakthroughs that have made deep neural networks a critical component of computing. And that was very very true when he won the award, and I think it's even more true today. I was actually lucky enough to be on hand for the ceremony, which was very very cool. And so arguably winning a Turing Award puts him at the top of academic computer science. But he's now also atop the business world. As of three weeks ago, he is the founder of a unicorn company. That means that it's valued at over a billion dollars—and multiple times more than a billion in this case. The company's called AMI Labs and maybe we'll hear a little bit about that. The name of the company stands for Advanced Machine Intelligence, but it's also the French word for friend. It's very clever. So, we are absolutely delighted that Yann accepted our invitation to speak to you today. I don't know what he plans to speak about, but I am confident that he will give you something to think about and talk about for days to come. Let's welcome Yann LeCun. Thank you so much, Michael. So one thing you have to realize is that I've become somewhat famous or infamous for bashing a particular... I'm going to go back to the title slide... bashing a particular aspect of machine learning called reinforcement learning that Michael is actually one of the, you know, pillars of. And so he's saying all those nice things about me despite the fact that I've been sort of dissing a lot of the field he's been working on. But in a nice way, right. And we overlapped in New Jersey, Michael and I, for a few years when he was at Bellcore and I was at Bell Labs. They were kind of sister or brother labs, I'm not sure how to say that. And later when we both joined AT&T Labs, we worked on AI although in different locations, but in the late 90s AT&T was probably one of the strongest places anywhere to work on AI. So it's not a completely recent phenomenon that industry research on AI is... I mean industry is kind of a dominant force in AI research. Okay, so this is a session where I'm supposed to give a short talk. I'm going to attempt to do this. I'm not familiar with the concept. But so that we can have a discussion afterwards, a fireside chat and question and answers. So first of all, thank you for inviting me; it was a pleasure. And I'm going to talk about world models and about the next generation AI systems. And I'll start by saying a slide that you just saw because Michael inadvertently flipped the slide. And it's like "AI sucks." You know, Michael said sometimes that I have controversial opinions; that's potentially one of them. So we have AI systems that can write code, they can pass the bar exam, they can win international math olympiads, but where is my domestic robot? Where is my robot that can, you know, clean the house, or learn to drive in 20 hours of practice like a teenager? We have systems that can manipulate language and they fool us into thinking they are smart because they manipulate language, but in fact they are completely helpless when it comes to the physical world. They can't really handle—LLMs in particular cannot really handle—high-dimensional continuous data, particularly if it's noisy. Think video or any kind of sensor data. Images, video, audio, sensor inputs from measurements, whatever. Financial data, scientific data. And the result is that everybody these days in AI is talking about agentic systems, so systems that can produce actions in the world. And almost none of those systems at the moment are capable of predicting the outcome or the result—the consequences—of their actions. It's a very bad way to produce an action if you're not able to predict the consequences of it. In fact, it might be dangerous. And so I don't know for the life of me why people believe that they're going to be able to build agentic systems by just training them to imitate humans to accomplish tasks, without those systems having the ability to predict the consequences of their actions in advance. And that's basically what world models are all about. The other question is: why can't AI systems solve new problems zero shot? If you ask them to accomplish a task they've not been explicitly trained to accomplish, they're not going to be able to do it. So all the robots you see doing amazing things today, they've been trained to imitate humans with enormous amounts of teleoperation data. Or you see robots doing kung fu dances or whatever, and that's super simple because the only thing you need to know is the dynamics of the robot. You don't need to understand how they interact with the world, and that's where things become complicated. Why can't AI have common sense and understand the real world? Like not like a human, but like your cat. So never mind humans, what about cats and dogs? They can do amazing things, they understand the physical world, they have some level of common sense. Certainly dogs do. And you know robots don't come close to this. As I said, 17-year-olds can learn to drive in a few hours of practice, and we have millions of hours of training data of cars being driven by people. We could use that data to clone the behavior of humans, training a neural net to basically behave like a human. Yet despite that, we don't have level five fully autonomous self-driving cars that are as reliable as humans. We do have autonomous driving cars; they're not level five. They can drive themselves pretty reliably, but they can also call home when they're stuck. Their zone of operation is limited, and they use all kinds of sensors and they require 15 years of very careful engineering to actually kind of do anything. I'm talking about Waymo. A big success of engineering, but hardly learning to drive in 20 hours of practice. So we're missing something big in terms of getting machines to learn as efficiently as humans and animals. And we keep bumping into this paradox that Hans Moravec, a roboticist, formulated in 1988. There are things that are easy for humans and difficult for AI, and they don't seem natural. The things that seem easy for AI are complicated for humans, like playing chess. But things we don't even consider intelligent actions, like grabbing an object or manipulating one, we can't do with AI yet. The real world is messy and the people who think that by training LLMs and making them bigger and training them on more language, we're going to reach human intelligence—they are deluded. Okay? Here is another controversial statement. Okay, the entire... there is literally hundreds of billions invested in an industry that basically is counting on the fact that LLMs are going to reach human-level intelligence. It's complete BS. It's useful, right? I mean it's not like those hundreds of billions are wasted. But we're not going to get human intelligence by just scaling up LLMs; it's just not happening. So how do humans and animals learn? We learn a lot by observation, of course quite a bit by interaction as well, and by imitation—by looking at our parents and other humans doing things. But some animals learn without actually the help of other members of their species and certainly not their parents. Think octopus; they never meet their parents. Think cuckoo birds; they never meet their parents either. So humans and animals learn mental models of the world, and in fact cognitive psychologists have tried to measure the type of concepts that infants are capable of learning in the first few months of life. So concepts, very basic concepts like object permanence—the fact that when an object is hidden behind another one, it still exists. We're not born with that; a baby learns this around the age of two to three months. What's kind of interesting is that notions like intuitive physics, like conservation of momentum or gravity, take about nine months for a baby to learn. If you show a six-month-old a scenario at the bottom of the slide here, where a car is put on a cart and is pushed off a platform and appears to float in the air, a six-month-old baby will barely pay attention. Whereas a 10-month-old will look at it with big eyes and really fixate because his or her world model is being violated. So I co-wrote a bit of a vision paper about this with Emmanuel Dupoux, who is a cognitive scientist who actually put together that chart, and Jitendra Malik who's a computer scientist and roboticist at Berkeley. So how do we reproduce this type of learning and what is intelligence really, right? There's all kinds of things that are being said about what it is to really be intelligent. And you would think that with the success of LLMs... you know, LLMs are good sort of interpolating information retrieval machines. If they are trained to answer a question, they will answer that question appropriately. They can solve certain problems because they have some special mechanisms that are built in when a particular problem is formulated. But they're not very smart; they can accumulate a huge amount of knowledge, and the reason why they require a large number of parameters and large memory is because they base it on the accumulation of factual knowledge. Essentially, there is not understanding—not much at least. Intelligence, in my opinion, is the ability to accomplish new tasks you've never been exposed to and solve new problems without any prior training or with minimal training. Like learning to drive in 20 hours of practice, not millions of hours of examples. So a fast adaptation in front of a new situation is intelligence. And it's not just adaptation; it's also creativity and the sort of extensions of adaptations if you want. Now the phrase artificial general intelligence or AGI makes absolutely no sense. And the reason is because it's designed to designate human-level intelligence, but human intelligence is highly specialized. We don't really realize that because all the problems we can apprehend are problems we can apprehend. But there's no question that we are very highly specialized. Now there is no question either that machines will eventually surpass humans in all domains where humans are intelligent. That's not going to happen next year, despite what Elon Musk has been saying for the last 15 years—that it is going to happen next year. And it's not going to happen in two years despite what Dario Amodei has been saying. It's not going to happen right away. At the very best, we might be convinced that we are on a good path towards human-level intelligence, but not yet at human intelligence, within five years. But it's going to take a while and it's almost certainly much harder than we think. In the past 70 years of history of AI, it's always been much harder than we thought. There's been about four or five waves—and Michael and I have been witness to this—over the course of the history of AI, of people who said: that's it, we've discovered the secret of intelligence, within 10 years the most intelligent machine on the planet will be a machine. And that proved to be wrong every single time; it's going to prove to be wrong this time around as well. I co-wrote a little philosophy piece about intelligence and how we should think about human-level or super-human intelligence. Now, why is it that human-level AI will require real-world data and sensory inputs as opposed to just language or text? Okay, take an LLM which is trained on all the publicly available text on the internet. That's typically something like 10 to the 13th or 10 to the 14th words. In the case of Llama 3, which was a model that my colleagues at Meta trained a couple years ago, that was about 2 times 10 to the 13th words. You turn these into tokens—a token is a subword unit—so that's 3 times 10 to the 13th tokens. A token is 3 bytes, so you get roughly 10 to the 14th bytes to pretrain your LLM on. That would take about 400,000 years for any of us here to read through that text if we read nine hours a day at 250 words per minute. Okay, a lot of data. Now take a human child, a four-year-old trained on visual sensory data. 16,000 hours of wake time in the first four years, which by the way is a small amount of video data—that would be about 30 minutes of YouTube uploads. We have two million optical nerve fibers going to the visual cortex from the retina carrying each about one byte per second. So that's about 10 to the 14th bytes in the first four years instead of 400,000 years. There is way more information getting to us through sensory inputs than there will ever be through language. And that tells you we're never going to get to human-level AI by just training on language; it's just not going to happen despite what you might hear. Now of course video data is much more redundant than language, but that's the point. Turns out all the machine learning procedures that we use rely on redundancy. If you try to train a machine learning system with completely random data, good luck. So what I've been working on over the last five years—but really the last 15 years—and recently created a company around is enabling the next AI revolution, which would be AI for the real world. This uses what's called self-supervised learning for high-dimensional continuous noisy data such as images, video, audio sensors, etc. And we want to produce AI systems that understand any environment or system that they need to control, including physical things so they understand the physical world, but also living things. We want systems that have persistent memory. Systems that can perform long chains of reasoning, can plan complex action sequences, possibly hierarchically. And systems that can accomplish new tasks zero shot without prior training and adapt quickly to new situations. And finally, systems that are controllable and safe. Current AI systems like LLMs are intrinsically unsafe. It doesn't matter too much because they're not very smart and we learn pretty quickly how to use them, but they can't be made completely reliable. Okay, so let me go into the weeds of it. There are two ways to build agentic systems. One is the way LLMs are built. You give them what's called a context or prompt, right? They observe something which is a sequence of symbols in the case of LLMs, and you train them to predict the next symbol. If those sequences of symbols represent a configuration of the world and then an action, they will attempt to predict the action. There's no reasoning in that. The way you trick an LLM or an autoregressive prediction system to reason is that you trick it into producing lots and lots of output tokens. And you hope that because it's going to spend more computation producing many tokens, it's going to turn into some form of reasoning. So you train it to go through steps of a reasoning and that's what they call reasoning. But it's not really what reasoning is. Reasoning is a search. When we reason, we search for a solution to a problem. In fact, that's the way classical reasoning in AI has been formulated since the 1950s. I give you a problem; the problem is whether a particular hypothesis can be a solution, maybe characterized by a function. Let's say your problem is to find the shortest circuit of a delivery truck through cities—the traveling salesman problem. It's a well-formulated problem, there is a well-formulated set of possible hypotheses, which is all the possible orders in which you can go through the cities, and then the function you need to minimize is the length of the path. And so you just search for a solution. In 1958, Newell and Simon, pioneers of AI, wrote a program that they very modestly called the general problem solver. And it was basically that: if you can characterize a solution to a problem and you have a search space for all the possible solutions, the only thing you need to do is search through the solutions for one that optimizes that function. They didn't realize yet, because computer science was not invented as a field, that most problems are exponential or NP-complete or NP-hard. But this concept that inference is a search through a set of potential solutions for a solution that minimizes a particular objective function—that is intrinsically more powerful than autoregressive prediction of tokens. Computationally it's intrinsically more powerful. You can reduce any computational problem to an optimization problem. You cannot reduce every computational problem to, or at least not efficiently, an autoregressive token discrete token prediction problem. Okay, so we have those two categories of AI systems. My money is on the second one. Okay? The one that performs inference by search. And that, plus a couple other ideas that I'm going to tell you about, led me to this long-term vision of a cognitive architecture whose centerpiece is a world model. So if you have a world model, what is it first of all? A world model is a predictive system that, given the current state of the world (or some abstract representation of it) and given an action that you imagine taking or intervention, can predict the next state of the world that will result. If you have such a world model that predicts what the world is going to be after you take an action, you can use that for planning. You can feed the resulting predicted state to an objective function that measures to what extent a task has been accomplished. By optimization, you can search for an action that minimizes this objective according to the prediction of your world model. You can also put guardrail objectives that are constraints the system has to satisfy in its optimization search, that guarantee that whatever actions are being taken, nobody will get hurt or the outcome will be good. Okay? So a system built around this blueprint is intrinsically safe because it cannot do anything but satisfy your guardrails according to its world model. Now of course the guardrails might be inaccurate, the model can be inaccurate, so I'm not saying those models are necessarily perfect. But at least by construction they will not knowingly produce actions that will produce dangerous results let's say. Now if you have a world model, the classical way to use them in optimal control—a very old engineering discipline—is that you can run your world model multiple times for multiple actions in a sequence of actions. Then you can plan that sequence of actions to arrive at a particular outcome. This is called MPC, model predictive control. Very classical. Except classically the model is written by hand. If you want to control an airplane, you can write down the equations of the airplane, the dynamics of the airplane, and that's your world model. Okay? You know the state of the airplane, you know the equation, you know the control variables, and so you can just do that. In fact, NASA has been using methods of this type to plan the trajectory of rockets since they've had their hands on electronic computers, which is going back to the 60s. Now really the way humans and animals perform planning is hierarchical. If I want to plan my return trip from here to New York, or in this example plan a trip from my office at NYU to Paris, I cannot possibly plan my entire trip at the lowest level of actions, which would be muscle control every millisecond. I can't plan my entire trip in terms of millisecond by millisecond muscle control. Not just because it's too long and complicated, but also because I don't have the information. I cannot plan the whole sequence of actions in advance. I don't know what the conditions are going to be when I get down on the street and I have to hail a taxi; how long am I going to wait? So all planning is hierarchical. We imagine high-level actions, like: to go to Paris, I know I have to get to the airport and catch a plane. Now I have a subgoal which is going to the airport. How do I go to the airport? I'm in New York so I can take a taxi or take the subway. Let's say I decide to take a taxi. How do I take a taxi? I need to go down on the street and hail a taxi because I'm in New York. How do I go down on the street? Well, I need to go to the elevator, push the button, then walk out of the building. How do I go to the elevator? I need to stand up from my chair, pick up my bag, open my door, close my door, walk to the elevator, avoid the obstacles, push the button. At some point down in the hierarchy, I'm going to have all the information I need to just act because I have the information, so I can plan low-level actions. But all of the planning we do is hierarchical. Now here is a big secret in AI: nobody knows how to do hierarchical planning. This is an idea; it's not been tested. We're working on it. But you should also work on it if you are interested in this question. Okay, so how are we going to train those world models? The natural idea here—everyone is talking about generative models—is we should train a generative architecture. All right? Let's say we want to train the system to predict what's going to happen in a video. Like you know LLMs are trained to predict what's going to happen in the text. You show it a piece of text and you ask it to predict the next words, right? There are forms of this where you take a text and you remove some of the words and you train the system to predict the words that are missing. But an LLM really just predicts future words. Why not do the same with video? Okay, take a video, show a system the first half, train it to predict the second half. I tried to do this for 15 years. The first 10 years of those 15 were basically a complete failure. And the reason was I was trying to predict pixels. Now as it turns out, when you're trying to predict what's happening in a video... you can, as it turns out, if you train a system to predict the next word in the text, do a decent job. You can never predict exactly which word will follow a sequence of words, but you can produce a probability distribution over all possible words in a dictionary. There's only a finite number. So it's just a big list of numbers between zero and one that sum to one. We know how to do that. But if you train the system to predict what's going to happen in a video, there is an infinite number of plausible futures. Most of the content there—most of the information in a video—is completely unpredictable. So if I take a video of this room, I point the camera to the left here, I turn the camera slowly, and then I stop. And I ask the system: predict the rest of the video. It's going to predict that the camera probably is going to keep panning. There is absolutely no way it can predict what all of you look like. No way. I mean that's impossible, right, at a pixel level. It can't predict the texture of the ground or the wood panels. It can't predict there is some sort of fireplace in the back. Like, okay, most of the information in the world is not predictable. But it's very often partially predictable. There is some abstract representation, a formulation of the state of the world that is predictable. So I can predict that it looks like a pretty full room and so most of the seats are going to be occupied. I can't predict what you look like, but a system might be able to predict there is one person in almost every seat. And it's probably going to predict at some point there is a wall, right, even if it can't predict the details. And we do this all the time. We predict things that are essentially not predictable. If I take this object, put it on my hand, and I tell you I'm going to lift my finger, you can tell what's going to happen. Okay? But if I repeat the experiment, it might fall in a different direction. So you cannot predict exactly at the pixel level what's going to happen. But at an abstract level, you can tell that this object is not stable and is going to fall in one of the two possible directions. So what does that mean? This means the idea that you're going to train a system to predict what happens in a video at the pixel level is simply not going to work, because the system is going to spend all of its resources trying to predict things it simply cannot predict. And in fact, we did that experiment. We had a bunch of papers going back 10 years where we tried to train a big neural net—I mean big for the time—to predict a few frames in the future of a video, and we got those blurry predictions because the system predicts the average of all the plausible futures. Some of my colleagues at FAIR also did this with simulated environments. It was complete failure. You know, eventually we figured out a way of making things a little better using latent variables to take care of the part of the video that is not predictable. But really it was kind of a failure except for very simple videos. So about five or six years ago, we came up with another idea. And that was derived from empirical results with attempting to use self-supervised learning for computer vision systems to understand images. And what we discovered was that the system architectures that worked the best were not generative architectures but what we now call joint embedding architectures. Okay, so you see the generative architecture on the left. Okay? You show it an X and you ask it to predict a Y, potentially conditioned on an action or something you know is happening. So X could be the beginning of a video, Y could be the continuation, or Y could be an image and X could be a corrupted version of that image or a different view of the same scene. Okay, and you're supposed to predict Y from X. And that doesn't work very well. A bunch of teams at FAIR and other places attempted to train things like variational autoencoders or masked autoencoders to learn representations of images, and the results were okay. It was super expensive computationally, but it didn't work very well. What actually works is joint embedding. So what is joint embedding? You take both X and Y and you run them both through encoders and you make predictions, but you make predictions in representation space. So the encoder applied to X produces a representation of X, SX, which does not contain all the information about X. And the encoder for Y produces a representation of Y, SY, which may not contain all the information about Y. And then you train a predictor to predict the representation of Y from the representation of X. This works really well. There is a lot of self-supervised learning procedures for image and video that actually use this type of architecture and they work really well. So there's a big issue though with this type of architecture. And the big issue is that the joint embedding architecture can choose to ignore X and Y altogether and minimize the prediction error by just producing constant SX and SY. So you have to find a way to prevent the system from collapsing. And this is the difficulty of training joint embedding architectures, particularly JEPA. So what is a JEPA? It's a joint embedding predictive architecture. It's a system of the type I just mentioned. You have variable Y you want to predict, variable X that you observe. You run them both through encoders which may or may not be identical, and you predict the representation of Y from the representation of X. You train the system by minimizing the prediction error. But you have to also ensure the system doesn't collapse. One good way to do this is to attempt to maximize the amount of information coming out of the encoders. That sounds great except we don't know how to measure information. We certainly don't know how to measure a lower bound on information content. We have all kinds of ways to measure approximate information and they are all upper bounds. When you want to maximize something, you need a lower bound so you can push up on it and the actual thing will go up. But we only have upper bounds. Okay, so what do we do? We come up with a good upper bound and then we cross our fingers. I'm sorry to say that's all we have. And there's very deep reasons why we can only have upper bounds to information content. We're never going to find lower bounds, okay? The reason is to measure information, you have to make some hypothesis about the type of dependencies that you allow your variables to have between them. And what that means is that you're going to ignore certain types of complex dependencies, which means that the measurement of information you're going to get is going to be over-estimated compared to the actual amount of information. At least if you believe you have objective measures of information, which actually I don't believe. So it's even worse than that. But there are methods to do this, and there is one called SigReg, which I'll say a few words about. And if you want to know more about SigReg, it's basically a kind of measure of information that's differentiable that you can use to maximize the information content coming out of a neural net or an encoder or any learning machine. This was at least in part invented at Brown and certainly implemented at Brown by Randall Balestriero together with me. Where are you, Randall? Right here. Okay, if you have any questions about this, talk to him. He's a new faculty in the CS department. And I proposed this JEPA architecture several years ago, maybe five years ago roughly. Since then there's been something like 1300 papers talking about JEPA on Google Scholar. So this is not just Yann's sort of obscure technique now anymore. Okay, so this idea that we need a system to learn abstract representations of observations to be able to make predictions is fundamental to intelligence and certainly fundamental to science. We do this all the time in science. In principle, I could explain everything that is taking place in this room here in terms of, let's say, quantum field theory. Any theoretical physicists here? Okay, one. Two. I mean, physicists will tell everyone here, you know, all of your fields are just applied physics to some level, right? Because I can reduce everything to physics. But in fact we don't, because it would be completely impractical to simulate the brain processes in sufficient detail to predict our reactions to this talk by doing quantum field simulation. So we invent abstractions: particles, atoms, molecules. In the living world: proteins, organelles, cells, organisms, individuals, societies, ecosystems. And every level of description in this hierarchy allows us to make longer-term prediction than the level below, and ignores a lot of the details about the level below. That's the essence of being able to understand the world. There is a wonderful quote from Albert Einstein, which is: "the most incomprehensible thing about the world is that the world is comprehensible." And it could be because there are those abstractions that we can derive that allow us to predict or explain certain behaviors of the world that would otherwise be way too complicated for us to understand. So the basic idea of understanding the world and modeling it and being able to make predictions is finding good representations, abstract representations that ignore the details that we cannot predict. That's exactly what JEPA does. And of course that's applicable to AI for science and things like this. World models should not be world simulators. They are approximations. They should not be digital twins; it's a very popular phrase at the moment. They certainly shouldn't be generative models because you don't want them to reproduce every detail of what you observe. They should not be video generation systems. A lot of people currently use the phrase "world model" to designate video generation systems. That's not what I'm talking about. What they should be is action-conditioned predictors in abstract representation space, preferably differentiable. Okay, so let me say I have a jet engine. A jet engine has typically a thousand sensors in it. Is there a way I can predict the state of the jet engine from the previous state and an intervention I'm making on it? Let's say it's a chemical plant or a power plant. Let's say it's a patient. Okay? I have a diabetes patient. Is there a way I can treat the patient with a course of treatment that will bring the patient to a good state with low blood sugar or whatever? So the point is not to predict every detail, but to predict enough that is predictable. Ignore the details you cannot predict. Same for a robot. We're not going to have useful robots until we have systems that basically can understand the physical world, and currently we don't. You see robots doing kung fu. It's super simple because the only thing that's involved is the dynamics of the robot itself, and that we can write equations for. But the interaction of the robot with an object for manipulating it, we don't know how to do that. This idea of a world model is really old; it goes back to the 1950s or 60s. 50s in the USSR, 60s in the US or in the West. The idea that you can use a world model to predict what's going to happen and then you can plan a sequence of actions as I was saying. It has been used by NASA since the 1960s. I wrote a couple vision papers about where I think AI research will go over the next few years. The one at the top is a long paper, relatively easy to read, that I wrote in 2022. And there are various versions of this talk, some slightly more technical than the present one. Yeah, let me go back to this if you want to take a picture. Okay. I'm going to use the last few minutes to talk about this SigReg idea and a model that Randall called LeWorldModel. I have no responsibility in the name. It's kind of a running joke because my boss at Bell Labs, Larry Jackel whom you know, called convolutional nets—back when I was at Bell Labs in the late 80s—he called it LeNet. Okay, and the name stuck and now I have to carry this "Le" everywhere. So the idea that Randall and I—mostly Randall—worked on is the idea that if you want to maximize the amount of information coming out of an encoder, you pass a bunch of samples through it and you're going to get a bunch of points coming out of it. And if you insist that those points form an isotropic Gaussian distribution, the variables are going to be independent of each other. Because in a joint Gaussian which is isotropic, individual variables are independent, right? So if you want to maximize information you have to make the variables independent of each other. And making the distribution isotropic Gaussian is a good way to do this. And it turns out you can do this differentiably. You can project the joint distribution over multiple axes in this potentially high-dimensional space. And what you get is a bunch of scalar distributions—one-dimensional distributions—and you can differentiably compute the distance between the Gaussian distribution for each of those projections and the empirical distribution. And you can move the points around so that they collectively become jointly Gaussian. And it works really beautifully and there's various mathematical tricks to do this. There are visualizations that Randall generated that show various methods, you know, turning a bunch of original points and moving them around so they become jointly Gaussian. And more recently together with Quentin Le Lidec who's a postdoc at NYU, Lucas Maes who's at Mila, and Damien Scieur, Randall and I worked on this thing called LeWorldModel. Okay, running joke. We can train a world model which is action conditioned and use it for planning for various tasks—you know, simple robotics tasks and everything. This needs to be scaled up, but we think we have a good handle. I'm not going to bore you with details because I'm running out of time. There are alternative approaches to this which have so far been scaled up more than this SigReg LeWorldModel method. One in particular called I-JEPA or V-JEPA or DINOv3, which is done by some of our colleagues at Meta, has lots of applications. Probably the best generic image feature extraction system in existence. But let me skip ahead a little bit if my computer agrees to, and talk about V-JEPA. This is a video prediction system which is not trained to predict in time. But you take a sequence of frames, you suppress some information about the sequence by blocking some parts of it—not just the future but like an entire chunk. And then you train one of those JEPA architectures to predict the representation of the full video from the representation of the partially masked video with various tricks. So it's not using SigReg or any kind of regularization of this type; it's using what's called a distillation method, which I'm not going to go into the details of. And you know, it's not entirely satisfactory in terms of the property of this algorithm, but it scales up and it works really well. What we have at the end, training this system on a relatively small amount of data... well, small amount, it's like 100 years of video, which basically is a day of YouTube uploads. Which is way more than any LLM has been trained on, but that can be trained on like 2000 GPUs in a few days. So it's not a gigantic LLM. Those systems actually have learned some level of common sense and intuitive physics. You show them a video—maybe a synthetic video—where something impossible occurs, like someone throws a ball and the ball disappears or it turns into a cube or it stops in the air. The internal prediction error shoots through the roof when those events occur because the system knows this just cannot possibly happen. So that's really interesting. It's the first time I see this kind of behavior. And I'm sorry the video somehow is not available because the video is on the web and I'm not connected. But anyway, let me conclude. I'm making a few recommendations here which make me extremely popular among some of my colleagues in machine learning, particularly in Silicon Valley. Okay? Abandon generative models in favor of those JEPAs. Abandon probabilistic modeling—I didn't have time to talk about this thing called energy-based model, which is basically the theoretical paradigm with which to understand all of this. Abandon contrastive methods in favor of regularized methods. I didn't talk about this, but those information maximization methods are basically a type of training for those energy-based models which are called regularized. And I put the last one just for Michael: abandon reinforcement learning. I mean really what I mean is not abandon it, just minimize its use because it's so damn inefficient. But you have to use it; you don't have a choice, right? Yann, thank you. That was fascinating. And as Michael predicted, you don't pull punches. That's terrific. Very candid. So the model predictive control algorithm was born at a refinery in Texas in the 60s and it's a wonderful example of algorithms and technology emerging from the private sector first and then the academics do the theorems and the proofs and show stability and robustness and all those kind of details. So if I count like how much time I have spent in academia and industry during my career, it's about half and half. Then in 2003 I became a professor at NYU full-time for about 10 years, and then joined Facebook at the time and was basically half and half between Facebook and NYU for a dozen years. They are complementary because the motivation for the people in those two environments is different. Right? In academia you want intellectual impact. In some parts of industry where you have ambitious research labs, you also have this ambition of having intellectual impact not just internal to the company you work for but also external. In industry you don't do this. If it doesn't move you forward in your main path, which is longer term, you just don't work on it because it's a waste of time. Okay? So there's the exploratory mode of research which is very much academic, and then there is the thing where you try to make progress on a long-term goal. In the early part of my career when I worked on convolutional nets and we applied it to character recognition—handwriting recognition—I had no interest in character recognition. It had to build itself. And then, you know, what better domain than perception? If you can do perception you can do a lot of things, and character recognition was pretty much the only perceptual task for which you could have data at the time. There was no USB camera or anything. So pick a long-term, very ambitious plan and work your way back and you're going to make progress—useful things are going to come out of that. Terrific advice. Terrific. So maybe delving in a little bit around some of the algorithms you talked about again. I'm reflecting back to things like model predictive control and even the applications you spoke of, aircraft engines, the medical problem—these are systems that we do have some rudimentary knowledge of, at least at the appropriate scale, as you correctly noted. So we can make sort of first principles or, as a lot of folks refer, physics-based models. Tell me a little bit about how you see intersections between how you bring causality and constraints versus how a PINN approach might do that. So causality... okay, the bad news is that in physics causality is not a well-established concept because all microphysics is time reversible, so causality does not exist in the first place, but at least until you start talking about thermodynamics or collective phenomena which seem to have a time direction. The I think an important point is there are a few, a small number of systems for which you can write down a small number of equations that are more or less solvable that will allow you to predict the behavior, right, of the system. So you want to predict the behavior of a small molecule interacting with another molecule, you do a simulation of what's called density functional theory. It basically models the density of electrons you know around a molecule and figures out like how they stick to each other. It's very expensive. You can only do it for small molecules unless you have gigantic clusters of GPUs. You can't do it to predict for example the conformation of a protein; it's just too complicated, right? So for a protein you need to have a specific model that has been trained from data to produce the right result. And of course those models incorporate some very deep knowledge about the nature of the problem. Like you know AlphaFold and ESMFold have you know some pretty clear way of representing the conformation of the protein to be able to make the prediction. So there's a lot of knowledge that is in there. But it's a very sort of high-level conceptual knowledge. It's not sort of very, very detailed to the point that you can reduce it to a small number of equations; you have to train the model to make the prediction. Most complex phenomena that we observe are of that nature where you cannot go from the microscopic to the mesoscopic because the underlying processes are so complicated. For example, you you know you might be able to write down a small number of equations that govern the behavior of a neuron in the brain, but that doesn't mean you understand the brain at the system level. Certainly not the learning mechanism. So this idea that you might be able with AI systems to learn a phenomenological model of a complex system that you cannot reduce to a small number of equations in the traditional reductionist approach, I think is very powerful. I'm sure there's a bunch of material physicists here. There's something called the magic angle. You take a monolayer sheet of graphene, okay, a single monolayer bunch of carbon atoms around a hexagonal structure. You put another sheet of the same stuff on top of it, you twist it by one degree, 1.1 degrees, and it becomes a superconductor. Why? We don't have like you know a reductionist explanation for this. There are theories, but there's a lot of people interested in you know developing phenomenological models of material properties that may help us discover you know new types of batteries, new ways to... there's some colleagues at Meta that have this really cool project where they're doing sort of DFT simulation and this kind of stuff using the supercomputers that Meta has access to to generate training data for a system to predict what is the interaction between a molecule of water with a particular material, whatever it is. With the idea that if you might use this model to design a particular material that would facilitate the separation of hydrogen from oxygen in water, you might have solved the energy storage problem. The reason why we can't cover a small desert with solar panels to produce electricity is that we need to store the energy somewhere. And separating hydrogen from oxygen in water is a very good way to do that if you can make it efficient. And you can only make it efficient if you have catalysis. You're a chemist, I'm not. You know catalysts that can do this, and basically AI can help us perhaps discover some new ones. That's a really interesting problem. They've actually kind of made a few proposals for that. Yeah, I mean I think you're raising really good points that the complexity, as you've invoked a couple times, sort of dictates how sophisticated the predictive model needs to be. Okay, so as I said, I think intelligence is not a collection of skills or an accumulation of knowledge, but an ability to solve new problems that you've never faced before. Let me take a concrete example. There is no way to design a set of questions or problems that the next generation of LLM will not be able to solve. And it's very simple. It's like as soon as you come up with a set of questions that LLMs cannot solve, you just integrate them in the training set and then the next generation is going to solve those questions. OpenAI has thousands of people actually kind of listing all of those questions that might be asked. And, you know, because I asked that question to Lex Fridman you had immediately hundreds of people trying that on ChatGPT and so now OpenAI has the data of people asking this question and of course they're going to train it to actually answer that question correctly. You know there's this example recently that someone—you know, there's someone that comes up with those those questions that LLMs don't answer properly. It said like, "I need to wash my car and the car wash is 100 yards from my place. Should I walk?" It's an abstract representation that serves the purpose of making predictions. And the state that you will use to make predictions in one millisecond with a one millisecond timeframe is not the same as the state you're going to use to make predictions an hour from now. And so what you need to—what the state, what the JEPA or similar model needs to represent by the state depends on the type of the horizon of the prediction they're supposed to do. The longer-term prediction you're making, the fewer details you can keep in the state because there are things you just cannot predict long term if there are too many details, right? You cannot predict the individual trajectories of molecules in this room. That's just too much computation, also measuring the the initial state would be would be very hard, impossible in fact. And very quickly the simulation will diverge from reality. But if your purpose is to, you know, predict the pressure in this room as a function of temperature you can do that PV=nRT. You don't need to simulate every individual molecule to be able to predict that. Or similarly, if you are an aircraft designer you want to design the airfoil of the wing of an airplane, you can do computational fluid dynamics. You don't simulate every molecule bumping into each other and onto the airplane. You model the state of the air in a little cube by velocity, pressure, and temperature and then you solve Navier-Stokes equations. Everything we do in prediction requires to find an abstract representation that eliminates all the stuff we cannot predict and allows us to make predictions at the time scale that we're interested in. And so what JEPA—the way JEPA is built—is that it finds this tradeoff between maximizing the information content of the representation but yet only preserving information you can predict. And then depending on the horizon at which you train the prediction, you will get different levels of details in the representation. Great advice. Terrific. How Bots, Deepfakes and AI Agents Are Forcing a New Internet Identity Layer | Alex Blania on a16z

a16z(260.0K) ·42:12 ·3.0K views · 2026-04-02
Alex Blania (Cofounder and CEO, Tools for Humanity and World) Ben Horowitz (Co-founder, Andreessen Horowitz (a16z)) Erik Torenberg (Partner, a16z)
As AI agents and deepfakes become indistinguishable from humans, World (formerly Worldcoin) is establishing a global identity layer using iris biometrics and privacy-preserving cryptography to verify "proof of human" uniqueness without compromising individual anonymity.
TL;DR As AI agents and deepfakes become indistinguishable from humans, World (formerly Worldcoin) is establishing a global identity layer using iris biometrics and privacy-preserving cryptography to verify "proof of human" uniqueness without compromising individual anonymity....

TL;DR

As AI agents and deepfakes become indistinguishable from humans, World (formerly Worldcoin) is establishing a global identity layer using iris biometrics and privacy-preserving cryptography to verify "proof of human" uniqueness without compromising individual anonymity.

The Identity Crisis: Why Current Proofs are Failing

The rapid evolution of AI has rendered traditional methods of verifying human identity on the internet obsolete.

  • Failure of Digital-Only Proofs: Methods like "Web of Trust" (analyzing social graphs or GitHub history) are no longer viable because AI can maintain long-term accounts, post regularly, and even vouch for other AI agents.
  • The Uniqueness Problem: Proof of human is a "1-to-N" problem (distinguishing one person from billions of others), whereas FaceID is a "1-to-1" problem (verifying you are the same person who owns the phone).
  • Mathematical Entropy: Common biometrics like fingerprints or facial recognition lack the mathematical complexity to distinguish individuals at a global scale. The iris is the only biometric with enough "entropy" to solve the uniqueness problem for billions of users.
  • AI Superiority in Persuasion: AI is becoming "superhuman" at programming humans. Blania cites studies where AI outperformed humans in changing minds on subreddits by analyzing user profiles to tailor psychological triggers.

Technical Architecture: The Orb and Privacy

To solve the privacy-vs-verification paradox, World utilizes custom hardware and advanced cryptography.

  • The Orb: A custom hardware device designed to prevent "replay attacks." It uses multiple sensors across the electromagnetic spectrum to ensure it is scanning a real, living human rather than a high-resolution display or deepfake.
  • Multi-Party Computation (MPC): To avoid a central database of biometric data, World splits iris codes into multiple pieces stored across different computers. No single entity possesses the full biometric "key."
  • Zero-Knowledge Proofs (ZKP): Users hold a secret on their phone that proves they are part of the verified uniqueness set without revealing who they are. This allows for fully anonymous authentication on third-party platforms.
  • Authentication Levels:
      • High-Level: Iris verification via the Orb.
      • Mid-Level: Face checks via smartphone (useful for rate-limiting but vulnerable to advanced deepfakes).
      • Legacy: Government ID integration using NFC chips, though this is less popular due to privacy stigmas.

Immediate Use Cases for Human Verification

The need for proof of personhood extends beyond social media bots to any high-stakes human interaction.

  • Dating and Social Media: Tinder is testing World ID in Japan to issue badges that signal high-level verification, ensuring users are not interacting with "catfish" bots.
  • Video Conferencing: As real-time deepfakes become photorealistic, high-value users (e.g., fund managers) will need cryptographic proof that the person on a video call is who they claim to be to prevent fraudulent wire transfers.
  • Gaming: Verifying human players is critical to preventing AI from destroying competitive integrity or economy-based betting markets.
  • Advertising Integrity: Content platforms like YouTube need to distinguish between human viewers and "YouTube farms" (thousands of phones watching videos) to maintain the value of ad impressions.

Scaling and Go-to-Market Strategy

The primary challenge for World is the physical distribution of hardware to reach a global audience.

  • The 15-Minute Target: Blania’s goal is to reduce the "average minutes to reach a device" to under 15 minutes globally. In the U.S., this requires deploying approximately 50,000 Orbs.
  • Distribution Partnerships: The company is exploring large-scale partnerships with retailers (e.g., Walmart or Starbucks) and even government entities like the DMV.
  • Orb-on-Demand: A new service is launching in the Bay Area where Orbs are delivered via motorbike to a user’s location for immediate verification, reducing the friction of finding a static location.

Jack Dorsey: Every Company Can Now Be a Mini-AGI

Sequoia Capital(134.0K) ·63:42 ·20.1K views · 2026-04-02
Jack Dorsey (CEO, Block) Roelof Botha (Partner, Sequoia Capital)
Jack Dorsey is transitioning Block from a traditional corporate hierarchy to a centralized AI "intelligence layer," reducing the workforce by 40% to eliminate "lossy" human management chains and refocusing the human role on high-level judgment, taste, and creative alignment.
TL;DR Jack Dorsey is transitioning Block from a traditional corporate hierarchy to a centralized AI "intelligence layer," reducing the workforce by 40% to eliminate "lossy" human management chains and refocusing the human role on high-level judgment, taste, and creative...

TL;DR

Jack Dorsey is transitioning Block from a traditional corporate hierarchy to a centralized AI "intelligence layer," reducing the workforce by 40% to eliminate "lossy" human management chains and refocusing the human role on high-level judgment, taste, and creative alignment.

The Shift: From Hierarchy to "Mini-AGI"

  • Information Legibility: Dorsey argues that traditional hierarchies exist primarily to manage information flow, which is inherently slow and "lossy." By capturing all digital artifacts (code, Slack, docs) into an intelligence layer, the company becomes a "world model" or "mini-AGI" that any employee or board member can query in real time.
  • The "Circle" Model: Unlike the "Pyramid" (Manager Mode) or "Flat" (Founder Mode), Dorsey proposes a circle with AI at the center and humans at the edge. The system provides the ground truth, while humans provide alignment and course correction.
  • Eliminating the Roadmap: Dorsey believes a company’s ultimate limiting factor is its fixed roadmap. In the new model, customer queries and proactive intelligence determine what is built in real time, shifting the company from "navigational interfaces" to "conversational capabilities."

Reimagining Organizational Roles

Block is normalizing its 6,000-person workforce into just three distinct roles to increase velocity and decrease organizational depth (aiming for 2-3 layers max):

  • Individual Contributor (IC): The builders and operators (engineers, designers, sales) who use AI agents to augment their breadth, allowing one person to do the work previously requiring a team of ten.
  • Directly Responsible Individual (DRI): The owners of customer outcomes who set strategy, assemble IC teams, and take full accountability for success or failure.
  • Player Coach: Replacing traditional managers, these individuals build human capacity and mastery. They do not just "tell" but "show" by doing the work alongside their teams.
  • CEO Mandate: The CEO's role shifts from decision-making to "architecting the intelligence" and ensuring the "edge" of humans remains aligned with the company’s values and "taste."

Executing the 40% Workforce Reduction

  • The Catalyst: The decision followed the late 2023/early 2024 release of advanced AI tools like Goose (a coding agent harness) and Cloud Code, which proved capable of understanding large, legacy codebases.
  • The "Shit Sandwich" Strategy: Dorsey and his team decided to execute a massive cut (40%) from a position of strength rather than reacting to market weakness.
  • The Math of Excellence: The team calculated the absolute minimum number of people required for three pillars:

1. Maintaining 100% uptime.

2. Full regulatory and legal compliance.

3. Growth and the transition to an AI-native structure.

  • Board Transparency: Roelof Botha noted the decision was supported because it was "principled and logical" rather than reactionary. Dorsey provided a detailed, logic-based manifesto to build trust before execution.

Leadership and Board Dynamics

  • The ALE Framework: Roelof Botha defines timeless CEO qualities through the acronym ALE: Authenticity, Logic, and Empathy.
  • Board Selection: Dorsey advises founders to treat investor selection as a "hire you can never fire." He suggests bringing in independent board members early (after product-market fit) to provide mentorship and a buffer between the founder and VCs.
  • Avoid the "Anti-Pattern": Dorsey strongly warns against being the CEO of two public companies simultaneously, calling it an "anti-goal" that leads to over-delegation and the accidental creation of disconnected holding companies.
  • Meditation as Training: Dorsey views meditation not as a "woo-woo" practice, but as physical training to sharpen focus, observe without reacting, and recognize the impermanence of business crises.

Notable Quotes

"Traditionally we've been relying upon humans in a management structure... to relay information. Instead, we can take all those artifacts and put an intelligence on top of it, build a model around it, and actually have a conversation with the company about how the company is doing." — Jack Dorsey
"It's not somebody who pounds the table harder who gets their product approved, it's just: more customers want this than that. That’s how we’re going to decide what to build next." — Roelof Botha
"If we knew that this was what our company was going to be in the future, I didn't want to have to do it with our backs against the wall... I want to be ahead of it because then we can do it with a lot more integrity and generosity." — Jack Dorsey
"I think AI is the biggest drainer of moats companies have ever seen. But some of the basics of dealing with people and leading remain true." — Roelof Botha

TL;DR

President Trump declared a decisive military victory one month into "Operation Epic Fury," claiming the near-total destruction of Iran's navy and air force while setting a two-to-three-week timeline to conclude objectives and urging global allies to secure their own energy routes.

Military Progress of "Operation Epic Fury"

  • Current Standing: One month into the conflict, President Trump asserted that U.S. forces have achieved "swift, decisive, overwhelming victories," claiming Iran's navy is destroyed and its air force is in ruins.
  • Nuclear and Missile Deterrence:
      • Operation Midnight Hammer: Trump highlighted previous B-2 bomber strikes on key nuclear facilities, stating those sites are now "dust" and under constant satellite surveillance.
      • Capabilities: Iranian ballistic missile and drone production have been "systematically dismantled," with analysts noting launch frequency has dropped to less than 10% of peak levels.
  • The "Short-Term" Timeline:
      • The President signaled the mission would be completed "shortly," specifically targeting a two-to-three-week window for final operations.
      • Contingency: If no diplomatic deal is reached, Trump threatened to strike Iran’s entire electrical grid simultaneously.

Shift in Global Alliances and NATO

  • NATO Criticism: Trump and Secretary of State Marco Rubio criticized the alliance as a "one-way street." Trump labeled NATO a "paper tiger" and "free riders," expressing disgust with members who refuse to designate the IRGC as a terrorist organization or allow the use of U.S. bases.
  • Middle East Partners: Trump explicitly thanked Israel, Saudi Arabia, Qatar, the UAE, Kuwait, and Bahrain for their support during the operation.
  • The Venezuela Precedent: Trump touted the rapid military takeover of Venezuela as a model of success, noting that the U.S. and Venezuela are now "joint venture partners" in oil production.

Energy Independence and the Strait of Hormuz

  • U.S. Energy Dominance: Trump emphasized that because of "drill baby drill" policies, the U.S. is the #1 producer of oil and gas and is "totally independent of the Middle East."
  • Responsibility Shift:
      • Trump told countries dependent on the Strait of Hormuz to "build up some delayed courage" and protect the passage themselves.
      • Economic Suggestion: He encouraged nations struggling with fuel costs to bypass the Middle East and buy oil directly from the United States.
  • Market Impact: Analysts Benham Taleblu and Hal Kempfer noted that the speech was designed to "hand-hold" global markets and calm fears of a "forever war," despite rising gas prices at home.

Post-War Strategy and Expert Analysis

  • Regime Status: Trump claimed that while regime change was not the official goal, it has effectively occurred because the original leadership is "all dead," replaced by a "less radical" group.
  • Strategic Risks:
      • The "Toll Booth" Problem: Benham Taleblu (FDD) warned against leaving Iran in control of the Strait of Hormuz, as they may implement a "toll booth" system to tax international commerce.
      • Chinese Involvement: Hal Kempfer expressed concern that a U.S. withdrawal from the Persian Gulf could open the door for increased Chinese naval involvement in the region.
  • Future Operations: Experts suggested the "shortly" timeline likely involves specialized raids or "mowing the lawn" strikes rather than a large-scale land occupation.

"He’s also trying to calm the markets... [Trump] made it very clear he does not like these long wars that draw our resources away." — Hal Kempfer, Retired Marine Intelligence Officer

The Orion spacecraft will orbit the Earth before slingshotting around the dark side of the moon 250,000 miles away. NASA's current timeline aims to put astronauts back on the moon in 2028, and long-term they hope to establish a permanent self-sustaining moon base. The moon is covered in water ice. Ken Gayley is here with me. He's an astronomy professor at the University of Iowa. Ken, we watched with excitement today as this thing was lifting off. Let's talk about the 10-day trip they have ahead of them. What are they going to be doing while they're in space? Yeah, well, you know, it was an amazing thing to watch. As an astronomer and also as I was one of those 11-year-old Mikes or Marys—I was eight when the Apollo 11 landed. So for me it was really a thrill to—to watch this. But, you know, as the astronomer in me, I—I'm very interested in what we can learn from the moon and from the travel to the moon. And this mission is very much a test mission. It's a mission where we're trying to see what it takes to go back to the moon. And it was Apollo 11 that landed, not Apollo 2 or 3 or something. So this is Artemis II. They're going to try to get there by Artemis IV. So it's already a relatively rapid version of the Apollo approach. But even so, they're taking it step by step and they want to learn as much as they can. So it'll be a 10-day mission and the astronauts will be very busy doing a lot of different things that involve learning what we need to know about surviving in space and doing scientific studies in space and a lot of the things about getting a rocket back to the moon, a technology that we had lost. So that's why this is so—so exciting for me as an astronomer. You know, we usually just look at the moon from the Earth, but they're going to get to see the moon from the backside. And that's nothing that no human eye has ever seen in entirety all at once—the entire moon. It's going to be an amazing thing for them to see. And then they're also going to try to learn—do some science and try to understand what's happening with the moon and what's happening with the radiation environment in space and many other things like that. Yeah, you mentioned the backside of the moon. Let's expand on that. They're heading to a specific part of the moon that we haven't been to before and they're doing that for a reason. What is the reason? Yeah, so it's very important to understand that this, as I said, is a test case. And so what it really is, it's kind of the first half of the full idea of the Artemis program. You know, a mission is you take off and then you come back. But actually if you want to go to the moon, you have to take off and then you have to land on the moon and then you have to take off again and then you have to come back. So this mission is not going to land on the moon. This is just the taking off and coming back part. The ability to actually land on the moon and take off again is a whole other thing. And that—we don't even have the technology for that yet, but we're building it. And you say, "Well of course we have the technology, we did it over 50 years ago." No, 50 years ago we landed at the Sea of Tranquility or other places near the equator of the moon that were very smooth and flat and comfortable and easier to get to and deal with. The real prize on the moon is the South Pole. The South Pole of the moon has craters that have ice in them—we heard about the water ice a moment ago. That's where a lot of it is, in these craters that never see sunlight. And then on the rim of these craters, there are places that see sunlight almost all the time. So you've got this remarkable combination of places where you can put a base and use solar energy almost all the time, and then a kilometer away you have an area of the solar system that has never seen sunlight, or at least not for an incredibly long time. And so you can do studies of what the solar system is like in places that don't get sunlight, and we just don't have access to that any other way. So the South Pole of the moon is the real prize. And you talk about the mission to Mars, you know, the moon as a stepping stone to getting to Mars. The idea there is to use the ice to make fuel. And that's going to be the South Pole as well. So that's really the new stakes: a permanent presence on the moon at a place where we can do studies that are impossible to do anywhere else and as a stepping stone to Mars. But this is a much more powerful rocket than anything we've launched recently, because we've only launched rockets recently for low Earth orbit. To get to the moon requires a much, much more powerful rocket. But the Saturn V had to be slightly more powerful even than this one, and that's because the Saturn V had to carry the lunar lander with it. And so it's important to understand that along with the different philosophy of this whole mission—which is to maintain a presence on the moon for a long time with a base—is that we can't carry the lander with us. It's sort of like a space station out in space on the moon. We have to put the lander in orbit around the moon first and then this rocket will go and rendezvous with the lander. And that is what they are doing right now; they're doing some space operations to see how they can maneuver in space. They're going to need to learn how to maneuver in space around the moon, rendezvous at the lander and then land from there. So this rocket only has to do the first half, which is getting to the moon and back. The other half of landing at the South Pole of the moon has to be done by a completely different rocket. And that—that is what is currently being built. So they're just anticipating that soon they will have this other rocket to use for the other half of the mission. So although it is a remarkably powerful rocket and much stronger than anything we've launched in a very long time, it's a different design from the Saturn V. Final question for you. There's been a lot made about—well, I could say this too, there was a lot made the last time we went to the moon about the space race and about the competition between the U.S. and—and—and really there was that competition that really—it ultimately—it sort of influenced and it really kind of boosted the level of curiosity and interest. Ultimately, that very much did play a role in getting us over the hump and getting us to the moon the first time around. This time, as you and I were discussing last night, it's not exactly the same situation. But there are reports that China has targeted a 2030 moon landing and a permanent outpost themselves. Is a permanent presence on the moon the new age version of the space race? Well, I think it certainly is being cast as that and that is the kind of rhetoric that we're hearing. Donald Trump (President of the United States)
