Hi, this is Steve Eisman, and welcome to another edition of the weekly wrap. Because of Good Friday, this is a shortened week. So this wrap is for the week ending Thursday, April 2nd, but recorded Wednesday, April 1. The first quarter is over, and it was a tough one. The market started the year with a nice January, and the market was up a bit over 1% by the end of January. However, largely because of the war, but also because of fears about the impact of AI and private credit, the quarter ended with the S&P down 4.6% for the year and Nasdaq down 7.1%. I will have more to say about the quarter and the performance of all the sectors and important sub-sectors on next week's wrap. But on this wrap, we will discuss the following: One, while the war in Iran continues to grab headlines, we need to keep focusing on the underlying economic issues at play. Two, more bad news about private credit. Three, we'll discuss the psychology and herd mentality of hedge funds. Four, the psychology of the executives of private credit funds and how similar that is to the psychology of Wall Street executives pre-the Great Financial Crisis. And finally, five, I answer two questions from viewers. So let's get started. We have now entered the fifth week of the war. Over the weekend, President Trump threatened to destroy Iran's energy infrastructure and Kharg Island unless the Iranians negotiate and reopen the Strait of Hormuz. Like I have said before, we are now in an environment where there is only one variable that matters: the war. On Monday, the market attempted to rally but ended down on the day after the publication of certain negative headlines. But on Tuesday, the market was up strong on a Wall Street Journal article which stated that President Trump told aides that he is willing to end the war without reopening the Strait of Hormuz. After our interview with Stephen Cook of the Council of Foreign Relations, which we posted on Monday of this week, I must say that that headline does not sound right. On the other hand, on the same Tuesday morning, President Trump put out a scathing statement on Truth Social. He said, quote, "All of those countries that can't get jet fuel because of the Strait of Hormuz, like the United Kingdom, which refused to get involved in the decapitation of Iran, I have a suggestion for you: Number one, buy from the US, we have plenty, and number two, build up some delayed courage, go to the Strait, and just take it. You'll have to start learning how to fight for yourself, the USA won't be there to help you anymore, just like you weren't there for us. Iran has been, essentially, decimated. The hard part is done. Go get your own oil!" unquote. By Tuesday morning, after the market soared almost 3%, President Trump asserted that there has been a regime change, suggesting the war is ending. Even given this and the Truth Social statement, I still find it hard to believe that President Trump will end the war with the Strait closed, because that would be viewed as defeat. But that's just my opinion. Clearly, making any investment decisions right now is very difficult, given that the markets are literally trading headlines. Moving on. More bad news on private credit. Shocking, I know. Monday morning, The Wall Street Journal published a very interesting article about private credit's exposure to the software sector, pointing out that the actual level of exposure is considerably higher than the 25% that has been estimated. By looking through the filings of scores of private credit funds, some commentators have reached that 25% estimate number. They say 25% of all direct lending loans are to software companies, specifically to software companies bought out by private equity from 2018 to 2022. The Journal article suggests that there is leeway in how private credit classifies a company as being or not being in the software sector. I call this data massaging. Private credit funds classify software companies that service other sectors, such as healthcare, as being in those sectors as opposed to software. Tomato, tomato. If that isn't data massaging, I don't know what is. The Journal looked at four major private credit funds and found the following: One, the Blue Owl Credit Income Fund, a $34.8 billion fund, reported 11.6% of its portfolio in software, but the Journal found its software exposure at around 21%. 11.6% versus 21% is quite a discrepancy. Two, the Blackstone Private Credit Fund, aka BCRED, an $82.5 billion fund, reported 25.7% in software, but the Journal found 33% exposure. Three, Ares Capital Corp, a $29.5 billion fund, reported 23.8% exposure, but the Journal found nearly 30% exposure. Finally, four, Apollo Debt Solutions Fund, a $25.1 billion fund, reported 13.6% software exposure, but the Journal found 16% exposure. What conclusion should we draw from this? It's pretty simple. Private credit's exposure to software is so large that the industry wants to massage the data to make it seem smaller. If and when the software industry suffers massive problems because of AI, this will have a huge and negative impact on private credit. Losses could be ugly. Now, do we care if a few private credit funds suffer huge losses? Yes, if it impacts the banks, who may or may not be involved in financing either the equity or the credit behind these deals, and if they take huge write-downs. And if pension funds across the country are invested and take a large hit, and if other entities with retail exposure like life insurance companies are invested and take a large hit as well, the impact will be felt. But more importantly, any sense of trust, safety, and security will be seriously eroded. Once the trust is broken, it's hard to restore. One more point. This software classification story is one more aspect of the opaqueness of private credit. We now know that private credit understates its software exposure. Also, the marks on its portfolio, the value of the loans, is priced by the private credit funds themselves. Now, how reliable is that? There is opaqueness in this industry everywhere you look, and that is part of the problem. Investors always fear what they don't know, and here, we don't know a lot. The role of psychology is a topic that does not get enough examination. The most you hear about it is when business news reports panic selling or sometimes panic buying of stocks. However, psychology plays a much larger role if you know where to look. We're going to discuss first the occasional herd mentality of hedge funds and then the role of arrogance in the realm of private credit. Let's first look at the herd mentality of hedge funds. The hedge fund industry likes to think of themselves as the smartest people in the room, the independent thinkers, the risk takers. And that is sometimes true. Here's what is also true: hedge fund managers and analysts know each other. They see each other at conferences. They go to idea dinners where they share long and short ideas. It can create a herd mentality in certain trades, and we can see that in the strange price movements of certain asset classes since the war in Iran began. Since the beginning of the war, oil prices and interest rates are up, but gold prices are down. Now, oil prices rising makes perfect sense. Interest rates rising is less intuitive. Normally, in a crisis, investors will flock to US Treasuries, thereby driving yields lower. This time, however, rates are up because higher oil prices have tapped into investor fears about inflation. Fair enough. Gold going down makes no sense. Gold is viewed as a hedge against the demise of fiat currencies, rising inflation, and an increase in overall risk. Normally, gold prices go up during a war. The combination of a war with rising inflation expectations should cause gold to skyrocket. Here we have a war and fears of inflation, and yet gold has declined about 10% since the war began. What explains this negative move in gold? Answer: herd mentality. Apparently, a bunch of major hedge funds, from Caxton to PIMCO to Citadel to Millennium and Balyasny, all had similar trades where they were betting on lower interest rates and higher commodity prices, including gold. When the war began, their interest rate bets went badly against them. All of the funds in these trades have suffered fairly significant losses since the war began. I'm guessing that the risk managers of these firms ordered all the portfolio managers with these trades on to reduce risk. That meant selling bonds and gold. And that's why gold prices are down when they should be up. It also could explain why interest rates have gone up so quickly. Now let's turn to the world of private credit. When I watch business news and see interviews of the executives of private credit, I'm struck by how almost universally they defend the industry. The defense is not surprising, but the tone somewhat is. Most of these executives are arguing that there is nothing wrong, just bad publicity. Since there is obviously something wrong, the question is whether they actually believe what they are saying. I'm convinced that they do. They actually believe that there is nothing wrong. There is an arrogance to their presentation, and that arrogance feels like the same arrogance Wall Street executives displayed before the Great Financial Crisis. History does not necessarily repeat, but it sure can rhyme. And here's the rhyme. Prior to the Great Financial Crisis, Wall Street executives suffered from one of the worst cases of arrogance in business history. And that requires an explanation. I'm creating a master class on how to analyze banks and lending companies, and I'll go into all of this in much more detail there. But here's the quick version. Banks make money with leverage. As long as they are making money, the more leverage, the more they make. The formula to understand is that Return on Equity equals Return on Assets times leverage. Imagine two banks, each with a 1% Return on Asset. If bank one is levered 10 times, its ROE is 10%. If bank two is levered 40 times, its ROE is 40%. From an economic perspective, both banks are equally profitable because they have the same exact Return on Assets. The only difference is the leverage. Historical footnote: between 1997 and 2007, leverage in the banks more than tripled, but the ROAs stayed flat. It was the leverage that caused Wall Street profits to explode. And you don't need to be a genius to take on more leverage. You just have to borrow, and anybody can do that. Wall Street executives are compensated, not on Return on Assets, but on a combination of net income and Return on Equity. Wall Street executives got paid tens of millions largely because they took on more leverage. An entire generation of Wall Street executives mistook leverage for genius. The resulting arrogance was on full display pre-crisis as executive after executive got on business news and claimed nothing was wrong, when in fact everything was wrong. Something similar has happened in the world of private credit, but the arrogance is not from leverage. Most private credit funds can only be levered two to one. The thing to focus on here is the lack of a credit cycle. Since the GFC, the level of losses in virtually all asset classes has been consistently low. Almost subterranean. It's been a great time to be a lender. What makes lending a unique business is that the cost of goods sold is unknown at point of sale. In the case of lending, the cost of goods sold is losses, and those losses always occur sometime in the future. When a lender makes loans, they underwrite those loans with a level of losses in mind. If losses come in lower, that creates excess profits. It's been 17 years since there have been losses in the United States, and private credit, as a result, has generated excess returns throughout. The lesson here is that an entire generation of private credit executives have mistaken a lack of a credit cycle for genius. That's why private executives believe that nothing is wrong. Because times have been good for so long that they have forgotten what it's like to experience serious levels of losses. It's been so long that it seems unimaginable. When private credit eventually blows up, it's going to be a shock to the industry's executives. Just like the GFC was a shock to Wall Street executives. It's not going to be pretty. Now I'm going to address two questions from viewers. First question is from Cormac, who says, "Hi Steve, massive fan of the show and the way that you conduct interviews, breaking down extremely complex information into something that even someone from a non-finance background can digest. I, however, have probably one of the dumbest questions you will receive. Is GameStop at its current price a value stock?" "I am not one of the people that would invest looking for short squeezes or other nonsense, but in this current environment where private credit is threatening the US economy, I see a stock with a massive stockpile of cash and wonder if it could be worth investing. I know that Michael Burry has mentioned that he sees some value in the stock, particularly if they use their cash to acquire other businesses with more upside and revenue. Thanks again for doing this show and being open to receiving questions." My response: without question, this is not a dumb question at all. Let's review a little history. GameStop operates a declining business. It sells video games in retail stores, but the entire business has moved online. In 2021, GME was a heavily, heavily shorted stock, but then it became a meme stock. Leaders of Reddit boards told their followers to engineer a short squeeze by buying GME. The stock soared multiple times and wiped out the hedge funds who were short. Literally wiped them out. That's the danger of being involved in a heavily shorted stock. Believe me, I know. You can be right on the fundamentals and still lose money. Topline fundamentals at GameStop have not gotten better, but the company has cut costs and so is profitable. Also, the company was very smart and used the price surge to raise capital. Currently, GME has 6.3 billion in cash and cash equivalents, but 4.1 billion in long-term debt, so it has net cash of 2.2 billion. The market cap is 10 billion. In a recent Substack, Michael Burry argued that GME has value because they could use their cash to acquire other businesses. I do not find this argument compelling at all. It sounds like a pipe dream to me. Buy a stock because they could buy something worthwhile at a good price? Good luck with that. Maybe they buy something good, and maybe they buy something not so good. Maybe they buy something at a good price, and maybe not. Too many maybes for me. Second question is from Maulin, who says, "Hi Steve, thank you for the great coverage of what is happening in the private credit markets over the last few weeks. It is my understanding that, within the private lending space, broadly speaking, there are two types of lending entities: a closed-end fund that is publicly listed, and retail investors can buy and sell at the prevailing price. It provides liquidity to retail investors and the issuing company is insulated from redemption pressure. I am talking about companies like OCSL." "The second vehicles, like BCRED, where there is no day-to-day price and limited (5% or so) volume, can be liquidated. However, their portfolio size is huge compared to listed BDCs. From the issuer's perspective, what is the attraction for BCRED-type non-listed vehicles? Is there more fee income in it? Why is the entire market not made up of companies like OCSL, OBDC etc? Thanks for great podcasts." Great question. The answer is all about who is investing in the private credit fund. Private credit funds, the ones that are not public, restrict investors to wealthy individuals and institutions by operating under exemptions from Investment Company Act registration. To qualify, they must, must restrict investors to accredited investors or qualified purchasers and limit marketing. In other words, to very rich people or institutions. This exemption allows them to avoid SEC registration as investment companies, which means no public reporting, no leverage limits under the '40 Act, and no liquidity requirements. The public entities that Maulin referenced are all public BDCs, Business Development Corporations. BDCs are a structure Congress created in 1980 to let retail investors access private credit. But in exchange, BDCs must register with the SEC, file public financials, and operate under leverage limits that private funds don't face. So BDCs are permitted to use leverage but only up to two to one. The non-public private credit funds do not face these leverage restrictions. Also, BDCs must distribute at least 90% of income. We discussed the war and how it will potentially change the region. We also discussed what his contacts are telling him about what is really going on. So check it out. TL;DR

President Donald Trump announced that "Operation Epic Fury" has effectively decimated Iran’s military, navy, and leadership within 32 days, neutralizing the regime's nuclear threat and asserting global energy dominance through U.S. production and a new partnership with Venezuela.

Military Progress: Operation Epic Fury

  • Operational Status: After four weeks of conflict, the President declared Iran’s navy destroyed, its air force in ruins, and the command structure of the Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps (IRGC) decimated.
  • Casualties and Leadership:
      • Regime Status: Claims most of the previous Iranian leadership is dead; a "new group" is reportedly more reasonable, leading to an effective, though unintentional, regime change.
      • U.S. Losses: Trump honored 13 American "warriors" who died during the operation, noting he had met their families at Dover Air Force Base.
  • Historical Comparison: Trump contrasted the 32-day duration of this conflict with the years-long timelines of World War II (3+ years), Vietnam (19+ years), and Iraq (8+ years), labeling it the most "devastating large-scale" loss for an enemy in history.

Strategic Objectives and Nuclear Non-Proliferation

  • Nuclear Facilities: Through "Operation Midnight Hammer," B-2 bombers have obliterated key nuclear sites. Trump emphasized that these sites remain under intense satellite surveillance to prevent any attempts at reconstruction.
  • Regional Context: The President justified the military action by citing 47 years of Iranian aggression, including the Beirut barracks bombing, the USS Cole attack, and the events of October 7th in Israel.
  • Ultimatum: If a deal is not reached within two to three weeks, Trump threatened to destroy Iran’s electric generating plants and oil infrastructure, which have been spared thus far to allow for potential rebuilding.

Global Energy and Economic Security

  • U.S. Energy Independence: Trump asserted that the U.S. is the #1 producer of oil and gas, producing more than Saudi Arabia and Russia combined.
  • The "Venezuela Joint Venture": Following a "quick, lethal" military intervention in Venezuela, the U.S. is now acting as a "joint venture partner" in the production of the world’s second-largest oil reserves.
  • Strait of Hormuz:
      • Policy Shift: The U.S. no longer requires oil from the Middle East and will not take oil through the Hormuz Strait in the future.
      • Responsibility: Nations that depend on the Strait must now take the lead in protecting it, or alternatively, purchase surplus oil from the United States.
  • Domestic Economy: Despite the conflict, Trump highlighted 53 all-time stock market highs in the past year and $18 trillion in record-setting investments entering the country.

Aerospace and Alliances

  • NASA Achievement: The briefing opened with a commendation for the Artemis II mission, noting the astronauts will travel further than any manned rocket in history, passing significantly beyond the moon.
  • Regional Allies: Trump explicitly thanked Israel, Saudi Arabia, Qatar, the UAE, Kuwait, and Bahrain for their cooperation during the conflict, vowing that the U.S. will not let them fail.

Iran's strategy was so obvious. They wanted to produce as many missiles as possible, and they did, with the longest range possible. They had some weapons that nobody believed they had. We just learned that and we took them out and we took them all out so that no one would really dare stop them in their race for a nuclear bomb—a nuclear weapon like nobody's ever seen before. They were right at the doorstep. For years everyone has said that Iran cannot have nuclear weapons, but in the end those are just words if you're not willing to take action when the time comes. As I stated in my announcement of Operation Epic Fury, our objectives are very simple and clear. We are systematically dismantling the regime's ability to threaten America or project power outside of their borders. That means eliminating Iran's navy, which is now absolutely destroyed, hurting their air force and their missile program at levels never seen before, and annihilating their defense industrial base. We've done all of it. Their navy is gone, their air force is gone, and their missiles are just about used up or beaten. Taken together, these actions will cripple the Iran military, crush their ability to support terrorist proxies, and deny them the ability to build a nuclear bomb. Our armed forces have been extraordinary. There's never been anything like it militarily. Everyone is talking about it and tonight I'm pleased to say that these core strategic objectives are nearing completion. As we celebrate this progress, we think especially of the 13 American warriors who have laid down their lives in this fight to prevent our children from ever having to face a nuclear Iran. Twice this past month I have traveled to Dover Air Force Base and it's been something. I wanted to be with those heroes as they return to American soil. I was with them and their families—their parents, their wives, their husbands. We salute them and now we must honor them by completing the mission for which they gave their lives. Every single one of the people, their loved ones, said, "Please sir, please finish the job." Every one of them. And we are going to finish the job and we're going to finish it very fast. We're getting very close. I want to thank our allies in the Middle East: Israel, Saudi Arabia, Qatar, the UAE, Kuwait, and Bahrain. They've been great and we will not let them get hurt or fail in any way, shape, or form. Many Americans have been concerned to see the recent rise in gasoline prices here at home. This short-term increase has been entirely the result of the Iranian regime launching deranged terror attacks against commercial oil tankers in neighboring countries that have nothing to do with the conflict. This is yet more proof that Iran can never be trusted with nuclear weapons. They will use them and they will use them quickly. It would lead to decades of extortion, economic pain, and instability worse than we can ever imagine. The United States has never been better prepared economically to confront this threat. You all know that. We built the strongest economy in history. We're going through it right now, the strongest in history. In one year we've taken a dead and crippled country—I hate to say that but we were a dead and crippled country after the last administration—and made it the hottest country anywhere in the world by far with no inflation. Record-setting investments are coming into the United States, over 18 trillion dollars, and the highest stock market ever with 53 all-time record highs in just one year. It all positioned us to get rid of a cancer that has long simmered. It's known as the nuclear Iran and they didn't know what was coming. They've never imagined it. Remember, because of our "drill baby drill" program, America has plenty of gas. We have so much gas. Under my leadership we're the number one producer of oil and gas on the planet, without even discussing the millions of barrels that we're getting from Venezuela. Because of the Trump administration's policies, we produce more oil and gas than Saudi Arabia and Russia combined. Think of that, Saudi Arabia and Russia combined, and that number will soon be substantially higher than that. There is no country like us anywhere in the world and we're in great shape for the future. The United States imports almost no oil through the Hormuz Strait and won't be taking any in the future. We don't need it. We haven't needed it and we don't need it. We've beaten and completely decimated Iran. They are decimated, both militarily and economically and in every other way. The countries of the world that do receive oil through the Hormuz Strait must take care of that passage. They must cherish it. They must grab it and cherish it. They can do it easily. We will be helpful, but they should take the lead in protecting the oil that they so desperately depend on. So to those countries that can't get fuel, many of which refused to get involved in the decapitation of Iran—we had to do it ourselves—I have a suggestion. Number one, buy oil from the United States of America. We have plenty. We have so much. And number two, build up some delayed courage. You should have done it before; should have done it with us as we asked. Go to the strait and just take it, protect it, use it for yourselves. Iran has been essentially decimated. The hard part is done so it should be easy. And in any event, when this conflict is over, the strait will open up naturally. It'll just open up naturally. They're going to want to be able to sell oil because that's all they have to try and rebuild. It will resume flowing and the gas prices will rapidly come back down. Stock prices will rapidly go back up. They haven't come down very much frankly. They came down a little bit but they've had some very good days over the last couple of days. We've done actually much better than I thought, but we had to take that little journey to Iran to get rid of this horrible threat. With our historic tax cuts where people are just now talking about receiving larger refunds than they ever thought possible, they are getting so much more money than they thought. That's from the great big beautiful bill. Our economy is strong and improving by the day and it will soon be roaring back like never before. It will top the levels that it was a month ago. I've made clear from the beginning of Operation Epic Fury that we will continue until our objectives are fully achieved. Thanks to the progress we've made, I can say tonight that we are on track to complete all of America's military objectives shortly, very shortly. We are going to hit them extremely hard over the next two to three weeks. We're going to bring them back to the Stone Ages where they belong. In the meantime, discussions are ongoing. Regime change was not our goal—we never said regime change—but regime change has occurred because of all of their original leaders' deaths. They're all dead. The new group is less radical and much more reasonable. Yet if during this period of time no deal is made, we have our eyes on key targets. If there is no deal, we are going to hit each and every one of their electric generating plants very hard and probably simultaneously. We have not hit their oil even though that's the easiest target of all because it would not give them even a small chance of survival or rebuilding. But we could hit it and it would be gone and there's not a thing they could do about it. They have no anti-aircraft equipment; their radar is 100% annihilated. We are unstoppable as a military force. The nuclear sites that we obliterated with the B-2 bombers have been hit so hard that it would take months to get near the nuclear dust, and we have it under intense satellite surveillance and control. If we see them make a move, even a move for it, we'll hit them with missiles very hard again. We have all the cards, they have none. It's very important that we keep this conflict in perspective. American involvement in World War I lasted one year, seven months, and five days. World War II lasted for three years, eight months, and 25 days. The Korean War lasted for three years, one month, and two days. The Vietnam War lasted for 19 years, five months, and 29 days. Iraq went on for eight years, eight months, and 28 days. We are in this military operation, so powerful, so brilliant, against one of the most powerful countries for 32 days. The country has been eviscerated and essentially is really no longer a threat. They were the bully of the Middle East but they're the bully no longer. This is a true investment in your children and your grandchildren's future. The whole world is watching and they can't believe the power, strength, and brilliance. They just can't believe what they're seeing. They leave it to your imagination, but they can't believe what they're seeing—the brilliance of the United States military. Tonight every American can look forward to a day where we are finally free from the wickedness of Iranian aggression and the specter of nuclear blackmail. Because of the actions we have taken, we are on the cusp of ending Iran's sinister threat to America and the world. And I'll tell you, the world is watching, and when we do, when it's all over, the United States will be safer, stronger, more prosperous, and greater than it has ever been before. May God bless the men and women of the United States armed forces and may God bless the United States of America. Thank you very much and good night.

WATCH LIVE: President Trump address to the nation — PBS News Special Report

PBS NewsHour(5.2M) ·27:06 ·252.1K views · 2026-04-02
Donald Trump (President of the United States)
President Trump declared significant military victory 33 days into "Operation Epic Fury," claiming Iran’s navy and air force are destroyed and outlining a final 21-day timeline to permanently eliminate the regime’s nuclear and missile capabilities.
TL;DR President Trump declared significant military victory 33 days into "Operation Epic Fury," claiming Iran’s navy and air force are destroyed and outlining a final 21-day timeline to permanently eliminate the regime’s nuclear and missile capabilities. Military Dominance:...

TL;DR

President Trump declared significant military victory 33 days into "Operation Epic Fury," claiming Iran’s navy and air force are destroyed and outlining a final 21-day timeline to permanently eliminate the regime’s nuclear and missile capabilities.

Operational Status of "Operation Epic Fury"

  • Military Dominance: President Trump asserted that in four weeks, the U.S. has decimated the Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps (IRGC) command and control.
      • Naval/Air Losses: He claimed Iran’s navy is "gone" and its air force is "in ruins."
      • Nuclear Neutralization: Strategic strikes (including "Operation Midnight Hammer" using B-2 bombers) have reportedly obliterated key nuclear sites to ensure Iran never obtains a nuclear weapon.
  • Timeline for Conclusion: The administration expects to complete all core military objectives within a two-to-three-week timetable.
  • Casualties: The President confirmed that 13 American "warriors" have died in the conflict so far, noting he has met with their families at Dover Air Force Base.

Strategic Shifts and Future Warnings

  • Targeting Infrastructure: If a diplomatic deal is not reached shortly, Trump threatened to hit all of Iran’s electric generating plants "simultaneously."
      • Oil Reserves: The U.S. has intentionally avoided hitting Iranian oil fields to allow for potential future rebuilding, though Trump noted they are the "easiest target of all."
  • Regime Change: Trump stated that while regime change was not the initial goal, it has effectively occurred because the original leadership is dead. He characterized the "new group" in power as "less radical."
  • Nuclear Surveillance: The U.S. is maintaining "intense satellite surveillance" over nuclear ruins; Trump warned that any attempt to recover highly enriched uranium will trigger immediate missile strikes.

Energy Independence and Global Impacts

  • Venezuela Intervention: Trump highlighted a recent, rapid military intervention in Venezuela, describing the U.S. and Venezuela as now being "joint venture partners" in oil production.
  • Domestic Energy: "Drill Baby Drill": The President emphasized that the U.S. is now the world's number one producer of oil and gas, producing more than Saudi Arabia and Russia combined.
  • Strait of Hormuz: Trump signaled a shift in maritime policy, stating that countries depending on oil through the Strait must "take the lead" in protecting it, as the U.S. no longer needs Middle Eastern oil.
  • Gas Prices: He attributed the recent spike in American gas prices to "deranged terror attacks" by Iran on tankers and predicted prices will drop rapidly as the conflict concludes.

Analysis and Context (PBS NewsHour Reporting)

  • Political Timing: Correspondent Liz Landers noted the address comes during an election year where the White House is under pressure regarding "affordability" (housing, groceries, and gas).
  • Justification for War: Reporter Nick Schifrin observed that this was a "case for war" delivered on Day 33 of the conflict rather than the day before, suggesting a move to bolster falling poll numbers.
  • Threat Imminence: Analysts pointed out that the President did not provide evidence of an "imminent threat" that triggered the initial strikes, an issue that previously led to the resignation of Joe Kent, former head of the Counterterrorism Center.

Watch Pres. Trump's full address on Iran from the White House

CNBC Television(3.4M) ·18:32 ·3.5K views · 2026-04-02
Donald Trump (President of the United States)
President Trump announced that "Operation Epic Fury" has effectively decimated Iran’s military and nuclear infrastructure within 32 days, while asserting that the U.S.
TL;DR President Trump announced that "Operation Epic Fury" has effectively decimated Iran’s military and nuclear infrastructure within 32 days, while asserting that the U.S. has achieved total energy independence through record domestic production and a new military-led "joint...

TL;DR

President Trump announced that "Operation Epic Fury" has effectively decimated Iran’s military and nuclear infrastructure within 32 days, while asserting that the U.S. has achieved total energy independence through record domestic production and a new military-led "joint venture" with Venezuela.

Execution of Operation Epic Fury

  • Military Dominance: President Trump reported that after four weeks of conflict, Iran’s Navy has been "destroyed," its Air Force is in ruins, and its command-and-control structures are decimated.
      • Leadership Attrition: Most of the regime’s original leaders are reported dead; Trump noted that while "regime change" was not the explicit goal, it has occurred de facto.
      • Tactical Scope: The operation has targeted the Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps (IRGC), missile launchers, and weapons factories.
  • Historical Comparison: Trump contrasted the 32-day duration of this conflict with longer historical wars (Vietnam, Iraq, WWII), claiming the U.S. has "eviscerated" a major threat in record time.
  • Human Cost: The President honored 13 American service members killed during the operation, stating that their families urged him to "finish the job" to prevent a nuclear Iran.

Energy Independence and Venezuela

  • The "Venezuela Venture": Trump announced that the U.S. military took control of Venezuela in "a matter of minutes." The U.S. is now operating as a "joint venture partner" with the country to produce oil and gas.
  • Global Production Leader: The U.S. now produces more oil and gas than Saudi Arabia and Russia combined.
      • Strategic Autonomy: Trump stated the U.S. is "totally independent" of Middle Eastern energy and no longer requires oil passing through the Strait of Hormuz.
      • Market Stability: While gasoline prices have risen recently due to Iranian attacks on commercial tankers, Trump predicted prices would "rapidly come back down" as the conflict concludes.

Nuclear Deterrence and Past Policy

  • Operation Midnight Hammer: Trump detailed a previous strike in June where B-2 bombers "obliterated" key Iranian nuclear facilities after the regime attempted to rebuild its program in secret.
  • Critique of Prior Administration: Trump reiterated his opposition to the 2015 nuclear deal, claiming the $1.7 billion in cash provided by the Obama administration was used to fund terror rather than buy loyalty.
  • Redline for Future Action: The President warned that if a new deal is not reached shortly, the U.S. will move to destroy Iran’s entire electric power grid and its remaining oil infrastructure.

Domestic Economic Strength

  • Economic Buffers: Trump attributed the ability to wage this campaign to the "strongest economy in history," citing 53 all-time stock market highs in a single year and over $18 trillion in new investments.
  • Tax Policy: The President highlighted that American citizens are receiving larger tax refunds than expected due to his administration’s "great big beautiful" tax bill, providing a financial cushion during the conflict.

Space Exploration Progress

  • Artemis 2 Launch: Trump opened the address by congratulating NASA on the successful launch of Artemis 2.
      • Mission Scope: The mission involves four astronauts traveling further than any human has ever flown, passing substantially beyond the moon before returning to Earth.

"We're going to hit them extremely hard over the next two to three weeks. We're going to bring them back to the Stone Ages where they belong."

Thank you very much. My fellow Americans, good evening. Let me begin by congratulating the team at NASA and our brave astronauts on the successful launch of Artemis 2. It was quite something. It will be traveling further than any manned rocket has ever flown and will very substantially pass the moon, go around it, and come back home from a distance that has never been done before. It's amazing. They are on the way and God bless them. These are brave people. We want to—God bless those four unbelievable astronauts. As we speak this evening, it's been just one month since the United States military began Operation Epic Fury, targeting the world's number one state sponsor of terror, Iran. In these past four weeks, our armed forces have delivered swift, decisive, overwhelming victories on the battlefield—victories like few people have ever seen before. Tonight, Iran's Navy is gone, their Air Force is in ruins, and their leaders—most of them—and the terrorist regime they led are now dead. Their command and control of the Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps is being decimated as we speak. Their ability to launch missiles and drones is dramatically curtailed, and their weapons factories and rocket launchers are being blown to pieces. There are very few of them left. Never in the history of warfare has an enemy suffered such clear and devastating large-scale losses in a matter of weeks. Our enemies are losing and America, as it has been for five years under my presidency, is winning and now winning bigger than ever before. Before discussing this current situation, I also want to thank our troops for the masterful job they did in taking the country of Venezuela in a matter of minutes. That hit was quick, lethal, violent, and respected by everyone all over the world. After rebuilding our military during my first term, we have by far the strongest military anywhere in the world and now we're working along with Venezuela and are, in a true sense, joint venture partners. We're getting along incredibly well in the production and sale of massive amounts of oil and gas—the second largest reserves on earth after the United States of America. We're now totally independent of the Middle East and yet we are there to help. We don't have to be there. We don't need their oil. We don't need anything they have, but we're there to help our allies. Tonight I want to provide an update on the tremendous progress our warriors have made in Iran and discuss why Operation Epic Fury is necessary for the safety of America and the security of the free world. From the very first day I announced my campaign for president in 2015, I have vowed that I would never allow Iran to have a nuclear weapon. This fanatical regime has been chanting "Death to America, Death to Israel" for 47 years. Their proxies were behind the murder of 241 Americans in the Marine barracks bombing in Beirut and the slaughter of hundreds of our service members with roadside bombs. They were involved in the attack on the USS Cole and they've carried out countless other heinous acts, including the blood—just horrible, bloody atrocities of October 7th in Israel, something that most people have never seen anything like. This murderous regime also recently killed 45,000 of their own people who were protesting in Iran. 45,000 dead. For these terrorists to have nuclear weapons would be an intolerable threat. The most violent and thug-ish regime on earth would be freed to carry out their campaigns of terror, coercion, conquest, and mass murder from behind a nuclear shield. I will never let that happen, and neither should any of our past presidents. This situation has been going on for 47 years and should have been handled long before I arrived in office. I did many things during my two terms in office to stop the quest for nuclear weapons by Iran. First, and perhaps most importantly, I killed General Qasem Soleimani in my first term. He was an evil genius, a brilliant person, and a horrible human being; however, he was the father of the roadside bomb and he lived just horrible for what he did. Iran would have been perhaps in a far better, stronger position had he lived. We would have had probably a different conversation tonight but, you know what, we'd still be winning and winning big. And then very importantly, I terminated Barack Hussein Obama's Iran nuclear deal—a disaster. Obama gave them $1.7 billion in cash—green, green cash. He took it out of banks from Virginia, D.C., and Maryland, all the cash they had. He flew it by airplanes in an attempt to buy their respect and loyalty, but it didn't work. They laughed at our president and went on with their mission to have a nuclear bomb. His Iran deal would have led to a colossal arsenal of massive nuclear weapons for Iran and they would have had them years ago. They would have used them; it would have been a different world. There would have been no Middle East and no Israel right now, in my opinion and the opinion of a lot of great experts, had I not terminated that terrible deal. I was so honored to do it; I was so proud to do it. It was so bad right from the beginning. Essentially, I did what no other president was willing to do. They made mistakes and I am correcting them. My first preference was always the path of diplomacy, yet the regime continued their relentless quest for nuclear weapons and rejected every attempt at an agreement. For this reason in June, I ordered a strike on Iran's key nuclear facilities in Operation Midnight Hammer. Nobody's ever seen anything like it. Those beautiful B-2 bombers performed magnificently. We totally obliterated those nuclear sites. The regime then sought to rebuild their nuclear program at a totally different location, making clear they had no intention of abandoning their pursuit of nuclear weapons. They were also rapidly building a vast stockpile of conventional ballistic missiles and would soon have had missiles that could reach the American homeland, Europe, and virtually any other place on earth. Iran's strategy was so obvious. They wanted to produce as many missiles as possible and they did, with the longest range possible, and they had some weapons that nobody believed they had. We just learned that out; we took them out and we took them all out so that no one would really dare stop them in their race for a nuclear bomb—a nuclear weapon like nobody's ever seen before. They were right at the doorstep. For years everyone has said that Iran cannot have nuclear weapons, but in the end those are just words if you're not willing to take action when the time comes. As I stated in my announcement of Operation Epic Fury, our objectives are very simple and clear. We are systematically dismantling the regime's ability to threaten America or project power outside of their borders. That means eliminating Iran's Navy, which is now absolutely destroyed. We are hurting their Air Force and their missile program at levels never seen before and annihilating their defense industrial base. We've done all of it. Their Navy is gone, their Air Force is gone, and their missiles are just about used up or beaten and taken out. Together these actions will cripple Iran militarily, crush their ability to support terrorist proxies, and deny them the ability to build a nuclear bomb. Our armed forces have been extraordinary. There's never been anything like it militarily. Everyone is talking about it and tonight I'm pleased to say that these core strategic objectives are nearing completion. As we celebrate this progress, we think especially of the 13 American warriors who have laid down their lives in this fight to prevent our children from ever having to face a nuclear Iran. Twice this past month I have traveled to Dover Air Force Base and it's been something. I wanted to be with those heroes as they return to American soil and I was with them and their families—their parents, their wives, the husbands. We salute them and now we must honor them by completing the mission for which they gave their lives. Every single one of the people, their loved ones, said, "Please sir, please finish the job." Every one of them. And we are going to finish the job and we're going to finish it very fast. We're getting very close. I want to thank our allies in the Middle East: Israel, Saudi Arabia, Qatar, the UAE, Kuwait, and Bahrain. They've been great and we will not let them get hurt or fail in any way, shape, or form. Many Americans have been concerned to see the recent rise in gasoline prices here at home. This short-term increase has been entirely the result of the Iranian regime launching deranged terror attacks against commercial oil tankers and neighboring countries that have nothing to do with the conflict. This is yet more proof that Iran can never be trusted with nuclear weapons. They will use them and they will use them quickly. It would lead to decades of extortion, economic pain, and instability worse than we can ever imagine. The United States has never been better prepared economically to confront this threat—you all know that. We built the strongest economy in history. We're going through it right now, the strongest in history. In one year we've taken a dead and crippled country—I hate to say that but we were a dead and crippled country after the last administration—and made it the hottest country anywhere in the world by far. We have no inflation, record-setting investments coming into the United States of over $18 trillion, and the highest stock market ever with 53 all-time record highs in just one year. It all positioned us to get rid of a cancer that has long simmered; it's known as a nuclear Iran. They didn't know what was coming; they've never imagined it. Remember, because of our "drill baby drill" program, America has plenty of gas. We have so much gas. Under my leadership we're the number one producer of oil and gas on the planet without even discussing the millions of barrels that we're getting from Venezuela. Because of the Trump administration's policies, we produce more oil and gas than Saudi Arabia and Russia combined. Think of that: Saudi Arabia and Russia combined. And that number will soon be substantially higher than that. There's no country like us anywhere in the world and we're in great shape for the future. The United States imports almost no oil through the Hormuz Strait and won't be taking any in the future. We don't need it. We haven't needed it and we don't need it. We've beaten and completely decimated Iran. They are decimated, both militarily and economically and in every other way. The countries of the world that do receive oil through the Hormuz Strait must take care of that passage. They must cherish it. They must grab it and cherish it. They can do it easily. We will be helpful, but they should take the lead in protecting the oil that they so desperately depend on. So to those countries that can't get fuel—many of which refused to get involved in the decapitation of Iran—we had to do it ourselves. I have a suggestion: number one, buy oil from the United States of America. We have plenty; we have so much. And number two, build up some delayed courage. You should've done it before; you should've done it with us as we asked. Go to the strait and just take it, protect it, and use it for yourselves. Iran has been essentially decimated. The hard part is done so it should be easy. And in any event, when this conflict is over the strait will open up naturally. It'll just open up naturally. They're going to want to be able to sell oil because that's all they have to try and rebuild. It will resume flowing and the gas prices will rapidly come back down; stock prices will rapidly go back up. They haven't come down very much frankly. They came down a little bit but they've had some very good days over the last couple of days. We've done actually much better than I thought, but we had to take that little journey to Iran to get rid of this horrible threat. With our historic tax cuts, people are just now talking about receiving larger refunds than they ever thought possible. They are getting so much more money than they thought. That's from the great big beautiful bill. Our economy is strong and improving by the day and it will soon be roaring back like never before. It will top the levels that it was a month ago. I've made clear from the beginning of Operation Epic Fury that we will continue until our objectives are fully achieved. Thanks to the progress we've made, I can say tonight that we are on track to complete all of America's military objectives shortly, very shortly. We're going to hit them extremely hard over the next two to three weeks. We're going to bring them back to the Stone Ages where they belong. In the meantime, discussions are ongoing. Regime change was not our goal—we never said regime change—but regime change has occurred because of all of their original leaders' deaths. They're all dead. The new group is less radical and much more reasonable. Yet if during this period of time no deal is made, we have our eyes on key targets. If there is no deal, we are going to hit each and every one of their electric generating plants very hard and probably simultaneously. We have not hit their oil even though that's the easiest target of all because it would not give them even a small chance of survival or rebuilding. But we could hit it and it would be gone and there's not a thing they could do about it. They have no anti-aircraft equipment; their radar is 100% annihilated. We are unstoppable as a military force. The nuclear sites that we obliterated with the B-2 bombers have been hit so hard that it would take months to get near the nuclear dust, and we have it under intense satellite surveillance and control. If we see them make a move—even a move for it—we'll hit them with missiles very hard again. We have all the cards; they have none. It's very important that we keep this conflict in perspective. American involvement in World War I lasted one year, seven months, and five days. World War II lasted for three years, eight months, and 25 days. The Korean War lasted for three years, one month, and two days. The Vietnam War lasted for 19 years, five months, and 29 days. Iraq went on for eight years, eight months, and 28 days. We are in this military operation, so powerful and so brilliant, against one of the most powerful countries for 32 days. And the country has been eviscerated and essentially is really no longer a threat. They were the bully of the Middle East, but they're the bully no longer. This is a true investment in your children and your grandchildren's future. The whole world is watching and they can't believe the power, strength, and brilliance. They just can't believe what they're seeing. They leave it to your imagination, but they can't believe what they're seeing: the brilliance of the United States military. Tonight every American can look forward to a day where we are finally free from the wickedness of Iranian aggression and the specter of nuclear blackmail. Because of the actions we have taken, we are on the cusp of ending Iran's sinister threat to America and the world. And I'll tell you, the world is watching and when we do, when it's all over, the United States will be safer, stronger, more prosperous, and greater than it has ever been before. May God bless the men and women of the United States Armed Forces and may God bless the United States of America. Thank you very much and good night.

Wealth Lessons: How Investor Jacob Walthour Makes Smart Money Moves

TL;DR Jacob Walthour, CEO of Blueprint Capital Advisors, emphasizes a strategic shift toward energy and AI infrastructure while warning of liquidity "cracks" in private credit, ultimately advocating for aggressive self-investment to navigate AI-driven career displacement. Top...

TL;DR

Jacob Walthour, CEO of Blueprint Capital Advisors, emphasizes a strategic shift toward energy and AI infrastructure while warning of liquidity "cracks" in private credit, ultimately advocating for aggressive self-investment to navigate AI-driven career displacement.

Investment Strategy & Market Outlook

  • Top Stock Pick (Nvidia): Walthour identifies Nvidia as the most accessible public play for the AI boom, as many other high-growth AI platforms remain in private venture capital stages.
  • The Energy Bull Case: High conviction in the energy sector due to the massive power demands of data centers, AI infrastructure, and Bitcoin mining.
      • Energy Consumption: One Bitcoin requires as much energy to produce as a single household consumes over 13 years.
      • Key Companies: Mentions Williams, Constellation, and NextEra as firms effectively investing in necessary infrastructure.
  • Private Credit Warning: Walthour warns of "cracks" in the private credit market following a decade of expansion.
      • Liquidity Mismatch: Investors are seeking redemptions that some large managers (citing Blue Owl as an example) are struggling to meet.
      • Tech Overexposure: He critiques the "miscalculated risk" of lending to tech companies that have failed to produce profits for 15–20 years.

Career Development & Financial Literacy

  • The $10,000 Investment: If given $10,000 today, Walthour advises investing it in personal training and AI certifications (e.g., from MIT or Harvard) to prevent being displaced by technological innovation.
  • Portfolio Philosophy: He differentiates between two distinct wealth stages:
      • The "Get Rich" Portfolio: Aimed at younger investors; focuses on high risk and maximum return.
      • The "Stay Rich" Portfolio: Aimed at older investors; prioritizes capital preservation and safety.
  • The Power of Persistence: Walthour recounts calling a contact at Lehman Brothers 14 times to secure his first internship, emphasizing that "iron sharpens iron" and the importance of mentorship in the "Wall Street game."

AI & Technological Disruption

  • Software Sector Risk: There is significant market suspicion that AI will "disintermediate" traditional software companies, leading to sell-offs even in companies with record performance.
  • Operational Efficiency: Walthour uses ChatGPT as a research and proofreading tool to shorten his research process.
      • The "75% Rule": He uses AI to get 75% of the answers he needs in 35 seconds, making him "smarter faster" while acknowledging the need for human fact-checking.

Blueprint Capital Advisors & Social Impact

  • Firm Profile: A Newark-based investment management firm with approximately $2 billion in total assets ($360 million discretionary; $1.36 billion non-discretionary as of March 2025).
  • Core Focus: Primarily focused on private credit and private equity (including venture capital, growth, and buyouts).
  • "People Over Profits": Walthour emphasizes the "ripple effect" of entrepreneurship, where firm success allows for recycling capital back into the community to support local organizations and economic solidification for families of color.
  • The Power 100: An initiative launched by Walthour to amplify "Black excellence" in asset management and create a resilient ecosystem against narratives that dismiss the contributions of women and people of color.

Notable Quotes

Any update there?" "No, those numbers are about right." "So, it's about right." "Yeah, those are about right. They don't change that often. Right? So started Blueprint Capital Advisors, myself and my co-founder, Carrie Pickett, it'll be 11 years ago in April. And you know, we had both worked for other organizations and we had both succeeded inside those organizations. But there's a reality to being a person of color on Wall Street, is that your contributions somehow never equate to, you know, your compensation." "And so we made the decision at a point in our career that why continue to do this for other people? Why continue to use our talents to enrich other people? Why not do it for ourselves? And in the process, be able to do it our way. Lead our own organization, hire the kind of people that we wanted to have in that organization. And then we could also do something with the capital that we weren't able to do when we worked inside of these larger organizations, and that is recycle it back into our community." "Right? And that's one of the things I learned from my parents very on because they were entrepreneurs and had a very successful business. You're able to solidify economically for your family everything that it needs, but you're also able to contribute to the local Boys Club, support the local Little League team, or help somebody who can't afford to pay for a funeral. I call it the ripple effect. You can do things that make it far more impactful than just you sitting in an office every day generating return on capital." "And that's what we were interested in doing. And so when we talk about Blueprint, we like to say we're not just about profits, we're about people. We're about helping you make money, but we're also about making the world a better place." "Yeah. Does it sit in the category of being a private equity firm or investment advisory firm?" "So we're an investment advisory firm, and as an investment advisory firm, we're focused primarily on private credit, and we also do private equity." "Private equity too. So private credit and private equity are two areas that you guys, you know, really focus on." "Correct." "No venture capital?" "Well, within private equity, there's venture capital, there's growth, and then there's leverage buyouts. And so all of that is within the work that we do within private equity." "Right. Every industry goes through a bear market and sometimes individual companies go through their own private bear markets when things go wrong. And we can sit here and talk about a dozen of them where that has occurred." "And so we have to expect that when you have over a decade of positive investment results and the kind of growth that you've experienced in private credit, that at some stage, you know, the party had to end. And when you see cracks in an asset class, that generally leads to people trying to head for the exit." "You know, they want to shoot first and ask questions later. And the challenge that you're facing right now in private credit is that investors expected that when they wanted to call their capital back, to redeem from those managers, they'd be able to get their capital back. And some of the most high-profile managers, some of the larger managers in the space, specifically Blue Owl, is having a challenge giving investors their capital back." "And you and I both know that if we went to the bank and we tried to get our money out and the bank told us we couldn't, the next thing you know there'd be a line around the corner. And so that's what you're starting to see in private credit; it is redemptions starting to accelerate because people are getting the sense that there's an asset-liability mismatch. I want my money back and for whatever reason, they can't give it back to me." "Yeah. You said the cracks. Is software a main contributor to those cracks? "Right? And that has all to do with this concern that AI is going to disintermediate the software space. "Yeah." Your 'first time' can shape democracy: the psychology of voting

World Economic Forum(1.1M) ·34:05 ·519 views · 2026-04-02
Michael Bruter (Director, Electoral Psychology Observatory at LSE)
Democratic participation is an intimate, relational experience rather than a purely rational one; the physical environment of voting and the "experiential" nature of a citizen’s first two elections are the primary determinants of lifelong engagement and political moderation.
TL;DR Democratic participation is an intimate, relational experience rather than a purely rational one; the physical environment of voting and the "experiential" nature of a citizen’s first two elections are the primary determinants of lifelong engagement and political...

TL;DR

Democratic participation is an intimate, relational experience rather than a purely rational one; the physical environment of voting and the "experiential" nature of a citizen’s first two elections are the primary determinants of lifelong engagement and political moderation.

The Impact of "Electoral Ergonomics"

The physical and social environment in which a vote is cast significantly influences voter behavior and psychological state.

  • Polling Station vs. Remote Voting: Voting in person fosters a sense of collective responsibility and "societal projection."
      • Extremism Risk: Research indicates that 18-to-24-year-olds are twice as likely to vote for extremist parties when voting remotely (e.g., at a kitchen table) compared to voting in a communal polling station.
  • Environmental Design: Most polling stations are repurposed schools or churches, but intentional design—such as soundproofing or specific booth views—can change how a voter relates to the act of voting.
  • Empathic Displacement: In a physical polling environment, voters are more likely to consider how their individual action interacts with the needs of the broader community.

The Critical Window of the First Vote

A citizen's early experience with democracy dictates their long-term participation habits.

  • Lifelong Participation: If an individual votes in one of their first two eligible elections, they are likely to become a lifelong participant. If these opportunities are missed, it is significantly harder to bring them into the democratic process later.
  • The "First Time" Milestone: Many young people experience their first vote as a deeply intimate milestone, comparing it to a first kiss or a first sexual experience rather than a mundane task like getting a driver’s license.
  • Lowering the Voting Age: Professor Bruter suggests that lowering the voting age to 16 can be effective because young people at that age are more willing to learn and discuss the process with family and teachers than they are at 18 or 19.

Hostility vs. Polarization

While political discourse often focuses on polarization, the psychological reality for younger generations is a distinct "hostility."

  • Defining Hostility: Young voters are not necessarily "polarized" (deeply loyal to one camp); instead, they are often hostile toward everyone, including those who vote for the same candidates.
  • Individual Sovereignty: Younger voters reject being "pigeonholed" into groups. They view themselves as unique, independent thinkers who want to make an individual contribution to a collective movement.
  • Inefficacy of Deliberative Democracy: Because young people value their individuality so highly, traditional "deliberative democracy" tools often fail to reduce their hostility.

The "Negative Mirror Perception" Problem

Social friction across generations is driven less by actual dislike and more by the false assumption of being disliked.

  • Mirror Perceptions: Most generations report liking and respecting each other. However, they suffer from "negative mirror perceptions"—the belief that the other generation views them with indifference, prejudice, aggression, or humiliation.
  • Disinhibiting Conflict: When people believe they are being judged or hated by another group, they feel justified in "defending" themselves, which leads to a cycle of alienation.
  • The Solution of Real-Life Dialogue: The most effective way to counter deepfakes, AI manipulation, and social hostility is to facilitate real-life, empathetic conversations between people with differing viewpoints.

***

Notable Quotes

"People voting in one of the first two elections of their lives will make them lifelong participants. If we miss one of those first two opportunities, it's going to be very difficult to bring them back to democracy." — Professor Michael Bruter
"Young people are not polarized; they are hostile. And what it means is that they're very negative towards people who vote differently from them, but they're almost as negative towards people who vote for the same political parties." — Professor Michael Bruter
"If [young people] vote remotely, from their kitchen table, they are twice more likely to vote for extremist parties than if they go to a polling station." — Professor Michael Bruter
"We need to stop assuming that people are more biased than us, more selfish than us, more apathetic than us. We need to give them the credit that they might be as good and well-intending as we are." — Professor Michael Bruter
Do you remember your first vote? More people compare their first vote to, say, their first sexual experience or their first kiss than they do to their first drive or their first job. All kinds of feelings that people have not just about democracy and voting, but also about different generations. It explores whether it matters if you're voting for the first time or if you're voting again. What was your first vote like? Will that determine how you will vote again? So, this concept comes from the idea that every generation disagrees with those who are older or younger than them. Let's say you're an older person and you might think, "Oh, these youngsters, they don't know what they're talking about." And vice versa, if you're a young person, you might think that your parents' views are not fashionable anymore. But this is not actually grounded in real disagreements. It's about the feeling that we have about others. And this idea of mirror perceptions, isn't it something to do with you thinking the other person dislikes you? Absolutely. So if I'm the young person talking to an older person, I'm thinking, "Oh, you don't like me because you think I'm a young snowflake, whereas you've been around the world." In fact, the other person might be thinking, "Oh, you don't like me for X and Y reason." So it's often that it's not necessarily my view of you, it's my view of what you think of me. This feeds onto each other. Then you're not starting from a place where you're neutral about each other. You already have a preconception which will for sure influence the way that you'll talk to that person.
