This is you speaking to London School of Economics Professor Michael Bruter. Thanks very much, Kateryna. Thank you. Hello, I'm Michael Bruter. I'm director of the Electoral Psychology Observatory and Associate Vice President for Research at the London School of Economics. So, how did you get interested in this work of connecting psychology of voting and our engagement in electoral processes? I think the reason why I've become so interested in understanding the psychology of voters and how voters simply experience democracy is because I think that in my field, which is the field of political behavior and electoral behavior, we've asked the wrong questions for a long time. We've asked the wrong questions because we've been very institution-centric. We've assumed that the only thing that mattered in an election is whether people vote or not and who they vote for. But those are the questions that really are of interest to political parties and politicians. They always want to know if they're going to win elections or lose elections. But for individuals, there are a lot more things that matter when it comes to democracy. For instance, can it enable us to live together? Can it resolve issues that we have with people who think differently from us? Can democracy make us happier? Can it actually intrude with our personal life and create some big disagreements or fights within families and between friends? So, all our work is about trying to take a very people-centric vision of democracy and elections, and it enables us to actually ask some very different questions. And an important concept that you develop in your book 'Inside the Mind of a Voter' is electoral ergonomics as well, right? Which explains this relationship. Can you tell me a little bit about it? What is important to know about this term? Sarah Harrison and I have developed one concept which is particularly relevant here, which is the concept of electoral ergonomics. It is a slightly different take from what people think of as electoral administration. We work with a lot of electoral commissions and international organizations like the European Union, Council of Europe, and United Nations. Very often, institutions think of elections as a process, and they are only worried about it as a self-contained process. We think of it as an experience instead. We think that the small details of the way in which democracy is organized will actually trigger different memories, personality traits, and emotions that people have but which are buried at the back of their minds. A lot of us have memories of democracy and elections from when we were little children. We weren't even able to vote, but we'll remember watching election night with our parents or going to the polling station with our parents. Many of us deep inside remember our first vote. And those things get triggered in different ways depending on the way you design a ballot paper or a polling station. Let me take the example of designing a polling station. The reality is that nobody, no country designs polling stations. What they do really is that they use schools, churches, or public buildings, and once every four or five years, they transform them into a polling station. Well, my brother is an architect. He would never design a hospital in the same way he designs a school. And so we started asking the question of what if you were to design an ideal polling station? What if you were trying to actually understand the functions that democracy plays in people's lives and actually design a polling station in a way that works best for those people? We realized that, for instance, if you actually have polling booths with a view, or if you have polling booths which are soundproof, people will actually behave in very different ways than if you just give them a curtain and a blind polling station or if there is a lot of noise around them. If you open the polling station for more hours, or if you actually vote on a different day, or design ballot papers in different ways, you are going to modify how people relate to the act of voting and to democracy. Michael, that is so interesting that you do talk about the environment, right? Very often, we don't think about that environment in which we vote or make such important decisions. We think that it is only a rational choice that we've made in advance and we've given it a lot of thought and then nothing else matters. Why is that not a very helpful way of thinking about voting? When we think about voting, we forget indeed to think about the environment in which people vote. And that's particularly relevant today because in many countries, in the face of declining levels of turnout, a lot of states have decided that maybe it's become too rigid to vote in the traditional ways. People are more mobile; they may live in different places or they might not be available on election day. And so they've encouraged the development of various forms of remote voting. We think of remote voting as being able to vote on a different day, in a different place, or on a different medium. The reality is that when you allow geographical remote voting—in other words, when people can vote on their kitchen table—that's going to be a very different experience from going to a polling station where you are surrounded by other voters. Your sense that you're doing something important is very different. Your sense that you're being connected with the rest of society during the election is very different as well. As a result, people are more likely to feel alienated and to be less projective in the way they vote. One of the concepts we've developed in 'Inside the Mind of a Voter' is the concept of societal projection and the concept of empathic displacement. What it means is that when people vote, they don't only conceive their vote as an individual decision. They try to understand how they relate to the rest of the country which is voting at the same time as them. A helpful way to think about it is if you think about when you were a kid and you might throw a chewing gum wrapper on the street, and your parents would say, "Well, you can't do that. You have to pick it up." Many kids will say, "Well, it's only a piece of paper. It doesn't matter." And your parents will say, "Well, imagine if everybody does the same." People are capable of that level of projection. They're capable of actually articulating how our individual action within a democratic context is going to interact and be either amplified or moderated by the action of other people around us. That's the reason why the environment matters so much because the environment will give us a very direct understanding of how the act of voting, our individual contribution to that democratic moment, is articulated with what millions of other people are doing at the same time. So then, is it fair to say that voting is very relational? We're thinking about how will my vote not only affect the result of the vote, but also how are my neighbors voting, are my parents voting, and how will I contribute to this specific election? Voting is eminently relational. It's both a very individual decision and a participation in a very collective moment. We need to stop thinking of those two things as contradictory because they're not. It's both very intimate and very collective. The articulation between the two takes place fully within the human mind. We're able to actually think of both the individual and collective dimension of the vote. One of the consequences of that, when it comes to remote voting, is that people experience elections differently depending on whether they vote remotely or in person. A very concrete example is that we found in our research that in the context of 18-to-24-year-olds, if they vote remotely from their kitchen table, they're twice more likely to vote for extremist parties than if they go to a polling station. This is true even after you control for their voting intention three weeks before the election. It's not about people self-selecting; it's about the fact that when you go to a polling station, you feel a sense of duty and a sense of importance. You sense a responsibility on your shoulders which you do not necessarily experience in the same way if you are only filling something on the internet. So you cannot take the social out of the voting basically, right? You can't take neither the social nor the individual out of the voting process and out of democracy. When you try to reduce people to groups, you are missing the very emotional and individual reality of the vote. But when you actually try to uproot voters and pretend that they're disconnected from how they perceive the rest of society, you're actually missing the meaning that they try to infuse in their democratic actions. People when they vote will have at the back of their minds the impression that they are part of a collective movement. On the contrary, they might feel they will be doing something which is going to alienate them from a large number of people who are either different from them or even similar to them. That will actually modify their perception of what they're actually doing and what their contribution is to democracy. Michael, during our conversation earlier, you mentioned first-time voters as kind of a separate category. In your book, you talk about first-time voters as a very important group. I was listening to a podcast where you gave an analogy of a bungee jumper. Why does it matter? Why should we help first-time voters get used to this reality of electoral processes? A lot of research focuses on the age of people and how young people tend to vote differently from old people. We focus a lot at EPO upon first-time voters. We think that beyond the question of age or generations, one of the elements of that is experiential. Somebody who votes is going to behave and think differently from somebody who doesn't. One of the analogies we use is the analogy to bungee jumpers. I've never bungee jumped in my life, but if I was going to do it for the first time, I would like the people who organize it to know that I'm a first-timer. I might have different questions and I might feel silly about asking them. There might be other people in the room who are exactly my age but are experienced bungee jumpers, and as a result, their relationship to that experience is very different. When it comes to first-time voters, we need to actually create a space where they feel that they are welcome as fully competent citizens. They should be treated differently from people who have voted many times because their questions and the importance of the moment will be very different to them. We can't have societies which tell people "Voting is important, democracy is important, you need to do it," and then, when they actually cast their vote for the first time, treat them as if nothing important was happening. That can create a sense of disillusionment and disappointment which can be very long-lasting. One of the findings we have is that people voting in one of the first two elections of their lives will make them lifelong participants. If we miss one of those first two opportunities, it's going to be very difficult to bring them back to democracy. That's why the first vote and the second vote are so unique. That is such an interesting finding. So having that beginning really matters and having the involvement at the beginning is the commitment. It helps people feel a part of a bigger whole and continue that trend throughout life. We think of the first vote as a "first time." When you're a young person and you want to know what it feels like to kiss someone for the first time or to have a job for the first time, you've got a lot of testimonies and impressions. You might not always ask your parents; very often you'll prefer to ask your siblings or your friends. We see that it's exactly the same with the first vote. Surprisingly enough, more people compare their first vote to their first sexual experience or their first kiss than they do to their first drive or their first job. Voting, being part of democracy, is a very intimate feeling for many young people. We need to respond to the excitement that many young people feel before going to vote for the first time. At the moment, when we try to make young people vote more, we try to guilt them into it. That doesn't work. We try to teach them as part of civic education, but people will become knowledgeable not because you teach them but because they have the right to vote. We shouldn't wait for them to know. The human mind is lazy by nature, so people only start learning things when they matter. One of our findings is that when you lower the voting age to 16, you increase the proportion of young people who vote and you give them the right at a time when they are much more likely to be aware of what matters and much more willing to learn about it. As you were describing how voting can be emotional, I was thinking about how social media is becoming a huge part of getting knowledge about the election and getting information for young people. What have you learned about the way that social media, psychology, emotion, and voting are interacting? A lot of people are very conscious of the fact that young people are very involved with social media and new technologies, including artificial intelligence. That worries a lot of people. Recent research has been dispelling a number of important myths, including on the so-called silo effects. The reality is that people don't necessarily live in as siloed a way as we thought that they did. Sometimes we exaggerate how gullible young people are on social media. The reality is that young people are actually quite critical. That comes from one important difference across generations which has to do with polarization. Polarization is what we hear about all the time, but one of the big findings of our research is that affective polarization—which is people disliking others who vote differently because they feel part of a camp—is yesterday's problem, or an old person's problem. Young people are not polarized; they are hostile. They're very negative towards people who vote differently from them, but they're almost as negative towards people who vote for the same political parties as them. They don't have a very strong sense of in-group political identity. They think of themselves as very unique, independent thinkers. This means that many of the solutions people have developed against affective polarization, things like deliberative democracy, do not work when it comes to hostility and do not really work when it comes to young people because they don't want the replacement of their individuality. That kind of going back to our point about being part of a bigger whole and being part of the social group, then that becomes more challenging, right? Because then young people maybe see themselves more individualistically. How... what are some of the solutions, do you think? I wouldn't say that young people are individualistic. In fact, there is a big prejudice among older generations that young people are selfish and apathetic. The reality is that they're neither of those things. But they feel that they are unique and they want to be part of something global. We need to understand that what they refuse is to be pigeonholed. They don't like it when you say "young people think this." What they want is to be able to make their own individual contribution to something very collective and social. They are collective and they care about the world and different generations. One of our big findings is that problems in our societies do not come from negative perceptions, but from negative mirror perceptions. In other words, young people claim that they like the old and they feel very close to them. Old generations say that they like young people and want to improve their lives. But everybody feels that other generations are negative towards them. We identified four big dimensions of that negativity: indifference, prejudice, aggression, and humiliation. We are fighting as democracies to dispel those perceptions of negative mirror perceptions. Negative mirror perceptions are very disinhibiting. If somebody was negative towards a different group, they would have to fight their own moral consciousness. But when people are acting on the basis of mirror perceptions, they feel they are barely defending themselves against a "ghost enemy." That's very disinhibiting because nobody could blame you for defending yourself. We need to dispel that mirror perception by being highly empathetic and proving to people that we do not dislike them. We need to show we respect their contribution even when they are very different from us. It is a very difficult thing to do, but it's very important. We tend to do that very well within intimate spheres, like families. People from different generations might argue, but usually they manage to understand that it's okay to disagree. But when it comes to social circles, it's much harder to articulate. So, if we imagine a dinner party where different, differently opinionated people come together, I suppose what you're describing is that they wouldn't even start talking to each other because they immediately assume that the other person thinks negatively or judges them. So where do we begin? One of the problems we face nowadays is that a lot of people are afraid of talking about politics. They worry that if people disagree with them, it's going to serve no purpose or, even worse, they are going to be met with aggression. Those things do happen in real life. But the best way to avoid prejudice and to correct distorting mirror perceptions is to talk to people who are very different from us. I've worked with various institutions when it comes to the impact of deepfakes and AI manipulation. I've advised the state of Arizona and some electoral commissions across the world. What we found is that the most effective way to avoid those problems is to ensure that people talk in real life with people who are different from them. When it comes to intergenerational differences, we need to remember what it felt like when we were young and for the first time we started having a voice. Some of us felt that when we started having that voice within our family or school, we were not taken seriously. Chances are that when we speak with young people today, our reactions might be giving them the same impression. That's what we call the cycle of alienation. The cycle of alienation is based on interviews we did with old German voters who felt frustrated that young people were not respecting them. Then they talked about their first experience of electoral hostility when they were very young and they were not taken seriously by their father or teacher. It was exactly the same situation described from two sides of the table. The problem we have as societies is that there is a certain lack of empathy. We forget to remember what it feels like to be on the other side of the table when we are on our particular seat at one given point in time. I find it fascinating, just how much empathy is required for all of us to be able to bridge this generational gap, it seems. A lot of empathy is needed, but a lot of empathy also needs to be expressed. Many of us paradoxically think that we are unique because most of us want to appease things. We think that we want to talk with people who are different from us, but then when we think about questions at the societal level, we expect the worst. We think that others are prejudiced or aggressive. The reality is that most people within democracies, even people who vote for political parties we don't like, are trying to be their best selves. That's one of the constant findings of our work. We've worked in 27 different countries recently and observed hundreds of elections, and we always have that same impression. Most people are genuinely trying to do the right thing. Empathy needs to start from that change of assumption. We need to stop assuming that people are more biased than us or more selfish than us. We need to give them the credit that they might be as well-intending as we are, and then the solutions will start replacing the problems. Thank you so much, Michael, for your time. It's a pleasure, thank you. Professor Michael Bruter was speaking to Kateryna Gordiychuk.
